10073-8{_}15{_}By GARLAND GREEVER      and      JOSEPH M. BACHELOR     TO  DANA H. FERRIN  WHOM THIS BOOK OWES MORE THAN A MERE DEDICATION CAN ACKNOWLEDGE     PREFACE  You should know at the outset what this book does _not_ attempt to do.
10073-8{_}16{_}It does not, save to the extent that its own special purpose requires, concern itself with the many and intricate problems of grammar, rhetoric, spelling, punctuation, and the like; or clarify the thousands of individual difficulties regarding correct usage.
10073-8{_}17{_}All these matters are important.
10073-8{_}18{_}Concise treatment of them may be found in THE CENTURY HANDBOOK OF WRITING and THE CENTURY DESK BOOK OF GOOD ENGLISH, both of which manuals are issued by the present publishers.
10073-8{_}19{_}But this volume confines itself to the one task of placing at your disposal the means of adding to your stock of words, of increasing your vocabulary.
10073-8{_}20{_}It does not assume that you are a scholar, or try to make you one.
10073-8{_}21{_}To be sure, it recognizes the ends of scholarship as worthy.
10073-8{_}22{_}It levies at every turn upon the facts which scholarship has accumulated.
10073-8{_}23{_}But it demands of you no technical equipment, nor leads you into any of those bypaths of knowledge, alluring indeed, of which the benefits are not immediate.
10073-8{_}24{_}For example, in Chapter V it forms into groups words etymologically akin to each other.
10073-8{_}25{_}It does this for an end entirely practical--namely, that the words you know may help you to understand the words you do not know.
10073-8{_}26{_}Did it go farther--did it account for minor differences in these words by showing that they sprang from related rather than identical originals, did it explain how and how variously their forms have been modified in the long process of their descent--it would pass beyond its strict utilitarian bounds.
10073-8{_}27{_}This it refrains from doing.
10073-8{_}28{_}And thus everything it contains it rigorously subjects to the test of serviceability.
10073-8{_}29{_}It helps you to bring more and more words into workaday harness--to gain such mastery over them that you can speak and write them with fluency, flexibility, precision, and power.
10073-8{_}30{_}It enables you, in your use of words, to attain the readiness and efficiency expected of a capable and cultivated man.
10073-8{_}31{_}There are many ways of building a vocabulary, as there are many ways of attaining and preserving health.
10073-8{_}32{_}Fanatics may insist that one should be cultivated to the exclusion of the others, just as health-cranks may declare that diet should be watched in complete disregard of recreation, sanitation, exercise, the need for medicines, and one's mental attitude to life.
10073-8{_}33{_}But the sum of human experience, rather than fanaticism, must determine our procedure.
10073-8{_}34{_}Moreover experience has shown that the various successful methods of bringing words under man's sway are not mutually antagonistic but may be practiced simultaneously, just as health is promoted, not by attending to diet one year, to exercise the next, and to mental attitude the third, but by bestowing wise and fairly constant attention on all.
10073-8{_}35{_}Yet it would be absurd to state that all methods of increasing one's vocabulary, or of attaining vigor of physique, are equally valuable.
10073-8{_}36{_}This volume offers everything that helps, and it yields space in proportion to helpfulness.
10073-8{_}37{_}Aside from a brief introductory chapter, a chapter (number X) given over to a list of words, and a brief concluding chapter, the subject matter of the volume falls into three main divisions.
10073-8{_}38{_}Chapters II and III are based on the fact that we must all use words in combination--must fling the words out by the handfuls, even as the accomplished pianist must strike his notes.
10073-8{_}39{_}Chapters IV and V are based on the fact that we must become thoroughly acquainted with individual words--that no one who scorns to study the separate elements of speech can command powerful and discriminating utterance.
10073-8{_}40{_}Chapters VI, VII, VIII, and IX are based on the fact that we need synonyms as our constant lackeys--that we should be able to summon, not a word that will do, but a word that will express the idea with precision.
10073-8{_}41{_}Exercises scattered throughout the book, together with five of the six appendices, provide well-nigh inexhaustible materials for practice.
10073-8{_}42{_}For be it understood, once for all, that this volume is not a machine which you can set going and then sit idly beside, the while your vocabulary broadens.
10073-8{_}43{_}Mastery over words, like worthy mastery of any kind whatsoever, involves effort for yourself.
10073-8{_}44{_}You can of course contemplate the nature and activities of the mechanism, and learn something thereby; but also you must work--work hard, work intelligently.
10073-8{_}45{_}As you cannot acquire health by watching a gymnast take exercise or a doctor swallow medicine or a dietician select food, so you cannot become an overlord of words without first fighting battles to subjugate them.
10073-8{_}46{_}Hence this volume is for you less a labor-saving machine than a collection and arrangement of materials which you must put together by hand.
10073-8{_}47{_}It assembles everything you need.
10073-8{_}48{_}It tags everything plainly.
10073-8{_}49{_}It tells you just what you must do.
10073-8{_}50{_}In these ways it makes your task far easier.
10073-8{_}51{_}_But the task is yours_.
10073-8{_}52{_}Industry, persistence, a fair amount of common sense--these three you must have.
10073-8{_}53{_}Without them you will accomplish nothing.
10073-8{_}54{_}Even with them--let the forewarning be candid--you will not accomplish everything.
10073-8{_}55{_}You cannot learn all there is to be learned about words, any more than about human nature.
10073-8{_}56{_}And what you do achieve will be, not a sudden attainment, but a growth.
10073-8{_}57{_}This is not the dark side of the picture.
10073-8{_}58{_}It is an honest avowal that the picture is not composed altogether of light.
10073-8{_}59{_}But as the result of your efforts an adequate vocabulary will some day be yours.
10073-8{_}60{_}Nor will you have to wait long for an earnest of ultimate success.
10073-8{_}61{_}Just as system will speedily transform a haphazard business into one which seizes opportunities and stops the leakage of profits, so will sincere and well-directed effort bring you promptly and surely into an ever-growing mastery of words.

10073-8{_}94{_}Reading Lists  INDEX     CENTURY VOCABULARY BUILDER   I      REASONS FOR INCREASING YOUR VOCABULARY   Sometimes a dexterous use of words appears to us to be only a kind of parlor trick.
10073-8{_}95{_}And sometimes it _is_ just that.
10073-8{_}96{_}The command of a wide vocabulary is in truth an accomplishment, and like any other accomplishment it may be used for show.
10073-8{_}97{_}But not necessarily.
10073-8{_}98{_}Just as a man may have money without "flashing" it, or an extensive wardrobe without sporting gaudy neckties or wearing a dress suit in the morning, so may he possess linguistic resources without making a caddish exhibition of them.
10073-8{_}99{_}Indeed the more distant he stands from verbal bankruptcy, the less likely he is to indulge in needless display.
10073-8{_}100{_}Again, glibness of speech sometimes awakens our distrust.
10073-8{_}101{_}We like actions rather than words; we prefer that character, personality, and kindly feelings should be their own mouthpiece.
10073-8{_}102{_}So be it.
10073-8{_}103{_}But there are thoughts and emotions properly to be shared with other people, yet incapable of being revealed except through language.
10073-8{_}104{_}It is only when language is insincere--when it expresses lofty sentiments or generous sympathies, yet springs from designing selfishness--that it justly arouses misgivings.
10073-8{_}105{_}Power over words, like power of any other sort, is for use, not abuse.
10073-8{_}106{_}That it sometimes is abused must not mislead us into thinking that it should in itself be scorned or neglected.
10073-8{_}107{_}Our contempt and distrust do not mean that our fundamental ideas about language are unsound.
10073-8{_}108{_}Beneath our wholesome dislike for shallow facility and insincerity of speech, we have a conviction that the mastery of words is a good thing, not a bad.
10073-8{_}109{_}We are therefore unwilling to take the vow of linguistic poverty.
10073-8{_}110{_}If we lack the ability to bend words to our use, it is from laziness, not from scruple.
10073-8{_}111{_}We desire to speak competently, but without affectation.
10073-8{_}112{_}We know that if our diction rises to this dual standard, it silently distinguishes us from the sluggard, the weakling, and the upstart.
10073-8{_}113{_}For such diction is not to be had on sudden notice, like a tailor-made suit.
10073-8{_}114{_}Nor can it, like such a suit, deceive anybody as to our true status.
10073-8{_}115{_}A man's utterance reveals what he is.
10073-8{_}116{_}It is the measure of his inward attainment.
10073-8{_}117{_}The assertion has been made that for a man to express himself freely and well in his native language is the surest proof of his culture.
10073-8{_}118{_}Meditate the saying.
10073-8{_}119{_}Can you think of a proof that is surer?
10073-8{_}120{_}But a man's speech does more than lend him distinction.
10073-8{_}121{_}It does more than reveal to others what manner of man he is.
10073-8{_}122{_}It is an instrument as well as an index.
10073-8{_}123{_}It is an agent--oftentimes indeed it is _the_ agent--of his influence upon others.
10073-8{_}124{_}How silly are those persons who oppose words to things, as if words were not things at all but air-born unrealities!
10073-8{_}125{_}Words are among the most powerful realities in the world.
10073-8{_}126{_}You vote the Republican ticket.
10073-8{_}127{_}Why?
10073-8{_}128{_}Because you have studied the issues of the campaign and reached a well-reasoned conclusion how the general interests may be served?
10073-8{_}129{_}Possibly.
10073-8{_}130{_}But nine times in ten it will be because of that _word_ Republican.
10073-8{_}131{_}You may believe that in a given instance the Republican cause or candidate is inferior; you may have nothing personally to lose through Republican defeat; yet you squirm and twist and seek excuses for casting a Republican ballot.
10073-8{_}132{_}Such is the power--aye, sometimes the tyranny--of a word.
10073-8{_}133{_}The word _Republican_ has not been selected invidiously.
10073-8{_}134{_}_Democrat_ would have served as well.
10073-8{_}135{_}Or take religious words--_Catholic, Methodist, Presbyterian, Episcopalian, Baptist, Lutheran,_ or what not.
10073-8{_}136{_}A man who belongs, in person or by proxy, to one of the sects designated may be more indifferent to the institution itself than to the word that represents it.
10073-8{_}137{_}Thus you may attack in his presence the tenets of Presbyterianism, for example, but you must be wary about calling the Presbyterian name.
10073-8{_}138{_}_Mother, the flag_--what sooner than an insult coupled with these terms will rouse a man to fight?
10073-8{_}139{_}But does that man kiss his mother, or salute the flag, or pay much heed to either?
10073-8{_}140{_}Probably not.
10073-8{_}141{_}Words not realities?
10073-8{_}142{_}With what realities must we more carefully reckon?
10073-8{_}143{_}Words are as dangerous as dynamite, as beneficent as brotherhood.
10073-8{_}144{_}An unfortunate word may mean a plea rejected, an enterprise baffled, half the world plunged into war.
10073-8{_}145{_}A fortunate word may open a triple-barred door, avert a disaster, bring thousands of people from jealousy and hatred into co√∂peration and goodwill.
10073-8{_}146{_}Nor is it solely on their emotional side that men may be affected by words.
10073-8{_}147{_}Their thinking and their esthetic nature also--their hard sense and their personal likes and dislikes--are subject to the same influence.
10073-8{_}148{_}You interview a potential investor; does he accept your proposition or not?
10073-8{_}149{_}A prospective customer walks into your store; does he buy the goods you show him?
10073-8{_}150{_}You enter the drawing room of one of the elite; are you invited again and again?
10073-8{_}151{_}Your words will largely decide--your words, or your verbal abstinence.
10073-8{_}152{_}For be it remembered that words no more than dollars are to be scattered broadcast for the sole reason that you have them.
10073-8{_}153{_}The right word should be used at the right time--and at that time only.
10073-8{_}154{_}Silence is oftentimes golden.
10073-8{_}155{_}Nevertheless there are occasions for us to speak.
10073-8{_}156{_}Frequent occasions.
10073-8{_}157{_}To be inarticulate _then_ may mean only embarrassment.
10073-8{_}158{_}It may--some day it will--mean suffering and failure.
10073-8{_}159{_}That we may make the most of the important occasions sure to come, we must have our instruments ready.
10073-8{_}160{_}Those instruments are words.
10073-8{_}161{_}He who commands words commands events--commands men.
10073-8{_}162{_}II      WORDS IN COMBINATION: SOME PITFALLS   You wish, then, to increase your vocabulary.
10073-8{_}163{_}Of course you must become observant of words and inquisitive about them.
10073-8{_}164{_}For words are like people: they have their own particular characteristics, they do their work well or ill, they are in good odor or bad, and they yield best service to him who loves them and tries to understand them.
10073-8{_}165{_}Your curiosity about them must be burning and insatiable.
10073-8{_}166{_}You must study them when they have withdrawn from the throng of their fellows into the quiescence of their natural selves.
10073-8{_}167{_}You must also see them and study them in action, not only as they are employed in good books and by careful speakers, but likewise as they fall from the lips of unconventional speakers who through them secure vivid and telling effects.
10073-8{_}168{_}In brief, you must learn word nature, as you learn human nature, from a variety of sources.
10073-8{_}169{_}Now in ordinary speech most of us use words, not as individual things, but as parts of a whole--as cogs in the machine of utterance by which we convey our thoughts and feelings.
10073-8{_}170{_}We do not think of them separately at all.
10073-8{_}171{_}And this instinct is sound.
10073-8{_}172{_}In our expression we are like large-scale manufacturing plants rather than one-man establishments.
10073-8{_}173{_}We have at our disposal, not one worker, but a multitude.
10073-8{_}174{_}Hence we are concerned with our employees collectively and with the total production of which they are capable.
10073-8{_}175{_}To be sure, our understanding of them as individuals will increase the worth and magnitude of our output.
10073-8{_}176{_}But clearly we must have large dealings with them in the aggregate.
10073-8{_}177{_}This chapter and the following, therefore, are given over to the study of words in combination.
10073-8{_}178{_}As in all matters, there is a negative as well as a positive side to be reckoned with.
10073-8{_}179{_}Let us consider the negative side first.
10073-8{_}180{_}<Tameness>  Correct diction is too often insipid.
10073-8{_}181{_}There is nothing wrong with it, but it does not interest us--it lacks character, lacks color, lacks power.
10073-8{_}182{_}It too closely resembles what we conceive of the angels as having-- impeccability without the warmth of camaraderie.
10073-8{_}183{_}Speech, like a man, should be alive.
10073-8{_}184{_}It need not, of course, be boisterous.
10073-8{_}185{_}It may be intense in a quiet, modest way.
10073-8{_}186{_}But if it too sedulously observes all the _Thou shalt not's_ of the rhetoricians, it will refine the vitality out of itself and leave its hearers unmoved.
10073-8{_}187{_}That is why you should become a disciple of the pithy, everyday conversationalist and of the rough-and-ready master of harangue as well as of the practitioner of precise and scrupulous discourse.
10073-8{_}188{_}Many a speaker or writer has thwarted himself by trying to be "literary."
10073-8{_}189{_}Even Burns when he wrote classic English was somewhat conscious of himself and made, in most instances, no extraordinary impression.
10073-8{_}190{_}But the pieces he impetuously dashed off in his native Scotch dialect can never be forgotten.
10073-8{_}191{_}The man who begins by writing naturally, but as his importance in the publishing world grows, pays more and more attention to felicities--to "style"--and so spoils himself, is known to the editor of every magazine.
10073-8{_}192{_}Any editorial office force can insert missing commas and semicolons, and iron out blunders in the English; but it has not the time, if indeed the ability, to instil life into a lifeless manuscript.
10073-8{_}193{_}A living style is rarer than an inoffensive one, and the road of literary ambition is strewn with failures due to "correctness."
10073-8{_}194{_}Cultivate readiness, even daring, of utterance.
10073-8{_}195{_}A single turn of expression may be so audacious that it plucks an idea from its shroud or places within us an emotion still quivering and warm.
10073-8{_}196{_}Sustained discourse may unflaggingly clarify or animate.
10073-8{_}197{_}But such triumphs are beyond the reach of those, whether speakers or writers, who are constantly pausing to grope for words.
10073-8{_}198{_}This does not mean that scrutiny of individual words is wasted effort.
10073-8{_}199{_}Such scrutiny becomes the basis indeed of the more venturesome and inspired achievement.
10073-8{_}200{_}We must serve our apprenticeship to language.
10073-8{_}201{_}We must know words as a general knows the men under him--all their ranks, their capabilities, their shortcomings, the details and routine of their daily existence.
10073-8{_}202{_}But the end for which we gain our understanding must be to hurl these words upon the enemy, not as disconnected units, but as battalions, as brigades, as corps, as armies.
10073-8{_}203{_}Dr. Johnson, one of the most effective talkers in all history, resolved early in life that, always, and whatever topic might be broached, he would on the moment express his thoughts and feelings with as much vigor and felicity as if he had unlimited leisure to draw on.
10073-8{_}204{_}And Patrick Henry, one of the few really irresistible orators, was wont to plunge headlong into a sentence and trust to God Almighty to get him out.
10073-8{_}205{_}EXERCISE - Tameness  1.
10073-8{_}206{_}Study Appendix I (The Drift of Our Rural Population Cityward).
10073-8{_}207{_}Do you regard it as written simply, with force and natural feeling?
10073-8{_}208{_}Or does it show lack of spontaneity?--suffer from an unnatural and self- conscious manner of writing?
10073-8{_}209{_}Is the style one you would like to cultivate for your own use?
10073-8{_}210{_}2.
10073-8{_}211{_}Express, if you can, in more vigorous language of your own, the thought of the editorial.
10073-8{_}212{_}3.
10073-8{_}213{_}Think of some one you have known who has the gift of racy colloquial utterance.
10073-8{_}214{_}Make a list of offhand, homely, or picturesque expressions you have heard him employ, and ask yourself what it is in these expressions that has made them linger in your memory.
10073-8{_}215{_}With them in mind, and with your knowledge of the man's methods of imparting his ideas vividly, try to make your version of the editorial more forceful still.
10073-8{_}216{_}4.
10073-8{_}217{_}Study Appendix 2 (Causes for the American Spirit of Liberty) as an example of stately and elaborate, yet energetic, discourse.
10073-8{_}218{_}The speech from which this extract is taken was delivered in Parliament in a vain effort to stay England from driving her colonies to revolt.
10073-8{_}219{_}Some of Burke's turns of phrase are extremely bold and original, as "The religion most prevalent in our northern colonies is a refinement on the principle of resistance; it is the dissidence of dissent and the Protestantism of the Protestant religion."
10073-8{_}220{_}Moreover, with all his fulness of diction, Burke could cleave to the heart of an idea in a few words, as "Freedom is to them [the southern slave-holders] not only an enjoyment, but a kind of rank and privilege."
10073-8{_}221{_}Find other examples of bold or concise and illuminating utterance.
10073-8{_}222{_}5.
10073-8{_}223{_}Read Appendix 3 (Parable of the Sower).
10073-8{_}224{_}It has no special audacities of phrase, but escapes tameness in various ways--largely through its simple earnestness.
10073-8{_}225{_}6.
10073-8{_}226{_}Make a list of the descriptive phrases in Appendix 4 (The Seven Ages of Man) through which Shakespeare gives life and distinctness to his pictures.
10073-8{_}227{_}7.
10073-8{_}228{_}Study Appendix 5 (The Castaway) as a piece of homely, effective narrative.
10073-8{_}229{_}(Defoe wrote for the man in the street.
10073-8{_}230{_}He was a literary jack-of-all-trades whom dignified authors of his day would not countenance, but who possessed genius.)
10073-8{_}231{_}It relies upon directness and plausibility of substance and style rather than temerity of phrase.
10073-8{_}232{_}Yet it never sags into tameness.
10073-8{_}233{_}Notice how everyday expressions ("My business was to hold my breath," "I took to my heels") add subtly to our belief that what Defoe is telling us is true.
10073-8{_}234{_}Notice also that such expressions ("the least capful of wind," "half dead with the water I took in," "ready to burst with holding my breath") without being pretentious may yet be forceful.
10073-8{_}235{_}Notice finally the naturalness and lift of the sinewy idioms ("I fetched another run," "I had no clothes to shift me," "I had like to have suffered a second shipwreck," "It wanted but a little that all my cargo had slipped off").
10073-8{_}236{_}8.
10073-8{_}237{_}Once or twice at least, make a mental note of halting or listless expressions in a sermon, a public address, or a conversation.
10073-8{_}238{_}Find more emphatic wording for the ideas thus marred.
10073-8{_}239{_}9.
10073-8{_}240{_}To train yourself in readiness and daring of utterance, practice impromptu discussion of any of the topics in Activity 1 for EXERCISE - Discourse.
10073-8{_}241{_}<Slovenliness>  Though we are to recognize the advantage of working in the undress of speech rather than in stiffly-laundered literary linens, though we are not to despise the accessions of strength and of charm which we may obtain from the homely and familiar, we must never be careless.
10073-8{_}242{_}The man whose speech is slovenly is like the man who chews gum--unblushingly commonplace.
10073-8{_}243{_}We must struggle to maintain our individuality.
10073-8{_}244{_}We must not be a mere copy of everybody else.
10073-8{_}245{_}We must put into our words the cordiality we put into our daily demeanor.
10073-8{_}246{_}If we greeted friend or stranger carelessly, conventionally, we should soon be regarded as persons of no force or distinction.
10073-8{_}247{_}So of our speech and our writing.
10073-8{_}248{_}Nothing, to be sure, is more difficult than to give them freshness without robbing them of naturalness and ease.
10073-8{_}249{_}Yet that is what we must learn to do.
10073-8{_}250{_}We shall not acquire the power in a day.
10073-8{_}251{_}We shall acquire it as a chess or a baseball player acquires his skill--by long effort, hard practice.
10073-8{_}252{_}One thing to avoid is the use of words in loose, or fast-and-loose, senses.
10073-8{_}253{_}Do not say that owning a watch is a fine proposition if you mean that it is advantageous.
10073-8{_}254{_}Do not say that you trembled on the brink of disaster if you were threatened with no more than inconvenience or comparatively slight injury.
10073-8{_}255{_}Do not say you were literally scared to death if you are yet alive to tell the story.
10073-8{_}256{_}EXERCISE - Slovenliness I  Give moderate or accurate utterance to the following ideas:  The burning of the hen-coop was a mighty conflagration.
10073-8{_}257{_}The fact that the point of the pencil was broken profoundly surprised me.
10073-8{_}258{_}We had a perfectly gorgeous time.
10073-8{_}259{_}It's a beastly shame that I missed my car.
10073-8{_}260{_}It is awfully funny that he should die.
10073-8{_}261{_}The saleslady pulled the washlady's hair.
10073-8{_}262{_}A cold bath is pretty nice of mornings.
10073-8{_}263{_}To go a little late is just the article.
10073-8{_}264{_}Another thing to avoid is the use of words in the wrong parts of speech, as a noun for a verb, or an adjective for an adverb.
10073-8{_}265{_}Sometimes newspapers are guilty of such faults; for journalistic English, though pithy, shows here and there traces of its rapid composition.
10073-8{_}266{_}You must look to more leisurely authorities.
10073-8{_}267{_}The speakers and writers on whom you may rely will not say "to burglarize," "to suspicion," "to enthuse," "plenty rich," "real tired," "considerable discouraged," "a combine," or "humans."
10073-8{_}268{_}An exhaustive list of such errors cannot be inserted here.
10073-8{_}269{_}If you feel yourself uncertain in these details of usage, you should have access to such a volume as _The Century Desk Book of Good English_.
10073-8{_}270{_}EXERCISE - Slovenliness II  1.
10073-8{_}271{_}For each quoted expression in the preceding paragraph compose a sentence which shall contain the correct form, or the grammatical equivalent, of the expression.
10073-8{_}272{_}2.
10073-8{_}273{_}Correct the following sentences:  The tramp suicided.
10073-8{_}274{_}She was real excited.
10073-8{_}275{_}He gestured angry.
10073-8{_}276{_}He was some anxious to get to the eats.
10073-8{_}277{_}All of us had an invite.
10073-8{_}278{_}Them boys have sure been teasing the canine.
10073-8{_}279{_}Another thing to avoid is triteness.
10073-8{_}280{_}The English language teems with phrases once strikingly original but now smooth-worn and vulgarized by incessant repetition.
10073-8{_}281{_}It can scarcely be said that you are to shun these altogether.
10073-8{_}282{_}Now and then you will find one of them coming happily as well as handily into your speech.
10073-8{_}283{_}But you must not use them too often.
10073-8{_}284{_}Above all, you must rid yourself of any dependence upon them.
10073-8{_}285{_}The scope of this book permits only a few illustrations of the kinds of words and phrases meant.
10073-8{_}286{_}But the person who speaks of "lurid flames," or "untiring efforts," or "specimens of humanity"--who "views with alarm," or has a "native heath," or is "to the manner born"--does more than advertise the scantness of his verbal resources.
10073-8{_}287{_}He brands himself mentally indolent; he deprives his thought itself of all sharpness, exactness, and power.
10073-8{_}288{_}EXERCISE - Slovenliness III  Replace with more original expressions the trite phrases (italicized) in the following sentences:  _Last but not least_, we have _in our midst_ one who began life _poor but honest_.
10073-8{_}289{_}After we had _done justice to a dinner_ and gathered in the drawing room, we listened _with bated breath_ while she _favored us with a selection_.
10073-8{_}290{_}_A goodly number_ of _the fair sex_, perceiving that _the psychological moment_ had come, _applauded him to the echo_.
10073-8{_}291{_}We were _doomed to disappointment; the grim reaper_ had already gathered unto himself _all that was mortal_ of our comrade.
10073-8{_}292{_}_No sooner said than done_.
10073-8{_}293{_}I soon found myself _the proud possessor_ of that for which I had acknowledged _a long-felt want_.
10073-8{_}294{_}After _the last sad rites_ were over and her body was _consigned to earth_, we began talking _along these lines_.
10073-8{_}295{_}With _a few well-chosen words_ he _brought order out of chaos_.
10073-8{_}296{_}The way my efforts were _nipped in the bud_ simply _beggars description_.
10073-8{_}297{_}I am somewhat _the worse for wear.
10073-8{_}298{_}Hoping you are the same_, I remain Yours sincerely, Ned Burke.
10073-8{_}299{_}Finally, to the extent that you use slang at all, be its master instead of its slave.
10073-8{_}300{_}You have many times been told that the overuse of slang disfigures one's speech and hampers his standing with cultivated people.
10073-8{_}301{_}You have also been told that slang constantly changes, so that one's accumulations of it today will be a profitless clutter tomorrow.
10073-8{_}302{_}These things are true, but an even more cogent objection remains.
10073-8{_}303{_}Slang is detrimental to the formation of good intellectual habits.
10073-8{_}304{_}From its very nature it cannot be precise, cannot discriminate closely.
10073-8{_}305{_}It is a vehicle for loose-thinking people, it is fraught with unconsidered general meanings, it moves in a region of mental mists.
10073-8{_}306{_}It could not flourish as it does were fewer of us content to express vague thoughts and feelings instead of those which are sharply and specifically ours.
10073-8{_}307{_}Unless, therefore, you wish your intellectual processes to be as hazy and haphazard as those of mental shirkers and loafers, you must eschew, not necessarily all slang, but all heedless, all habitual use of it.
10073-8{_}308{_}Now and then a touch of slang, judiciously chosen, is effective; now and then it fulfils a legitimate purpose of language.
10073-8{_}309{_}But normally you should express yourself as befits one who has at his disposal the rich treasuries of the dictionary instead of a mere stock of greasy counterfeit phrases.
10073-8{_}310{_}EXERCISE - Slovenliness IV  Replace the following slang with acceptable English:  We pulled a new wrinkle.
10073-8{_}311{_}He's an easy mark.

10073-8{_}314{_}I have all the inside dope.
10073-8{_}315{_}You can't bamboozle me.
10073-8{_}316{_}What a phiz the bloke has!
10073-8{_}317{_}You're talking through your hat.
10073-8{_}318{_}We had a long confab with the gink.
10073-8{_}319{_}He's loony over that chicken.
10073-8{_}320{_}The prof. told us to vamoose.
10073-8{_}321{_}Take a squint at the girl with the specs.
10073-8{_}322{_}Ain't it fierce the way they swipe umbrellas?
10073-8{_}323{_}Goodnight, how she claws the ivory!
10073-8{_}324{_}Nix on the rough stuff.

10073-8{_}326{_}<Wordiness>  As a precaution against tameness you should cultivate spontaneity and daring.
10073-8{_}327{_}As a precaution against slovenliness you should cultivate freshness and accuracy.
10073-8{_}328{_}But to display spontaneity, daring, freshness, accuracy you must have or acquire a large stock, a wide range, of words.
10073-8{_}329{_}Now this possession, like any other, brings with it temptation.
10073-8{_}330{_}If we have words, we like to use them.
10073-8{_}331{_}Nor do we wait for an indulgence in this luxury until we have consciously set to work to amass a vocabulary.
10073-8{_}332{_}Verbosity is, in truth, the besetting linguistic sin.
10073-8{_}333{_}Most people are lavish with words, as most people are lavish with money.
10073-8{_}334{_}This is not to say that in the currency of language they are rich.
10073-8{_}335{_}But even if they lack the means--and the desire--to be extravagant, they yet make their purchases heedlessly or fail to count their linguistic change.
10073-8{_}336{_}The degree of our thrift, not the amount of our income or resources, is what marks us as being or not being verbal spendthrifts.
10073-8{_}337{_}The frugal manager buys his ideas at exactly the purchase price.
10073-8{_}338{_}He does not expend a twenty-dollar bill for a box of matches.
10073-8{_}339{_}Have words by all means, the more of them the better, but use them temperately, sparingly.
10073-8{_}340{_}Do not think that a passage to be admirable must be studded with ostentatious terms.
10073-8{_}341{_}Consider the Gettysburg Address or the Parable of the Prodigal Son.
10073-8{_}342{_}These convey their thought and feeling perfectly, yet both are simple--exquisitely simple.
10073-8{_}343{_}They strike us indeed as being inevitable--as if their phrasing could not have been other than it is.
10073-8{_}344{_}They have, they are, finality.
10073-8{_}345{_}What could glittering phraseology add to them?
10073-8{_}346{_}Nothing; it could only mar them.
10073-8{_}347{_}Yet Lincoln and the Scriptural writers were not afraid to use big words when occasion required.
10073-8{_}348{_}What they sought was to make their speech adequate without carrying a superfluous syllable.
10073-8{_}349{_}"The sun set" is more natural and effective than "The celestial orb that blesses our terrestrial globe with its warm and luminous rays sank to its nocturnal repose behind the western horizon."
10073-8{_}350{_}Great writers--the true masters--have often held "fine writing" and pretentious speaking up to ridicule.
10073-8{_}351{_}Thus Shakespeare has Kent, who has been rebuked for his bluntness, indulge in a grandiloquent outburst:    "Sir, in good sooth, in sincere verity,   Under the allowance of your grand aspect,   Whose influence, like the wreath of radiant fire   On flickering Phoebus' front,--"  No wonder Kent is interrupted with a "What meanest by this?"
10073-8{_}352{_}Sometimes great writers use ornate utterance for humorous effects.
10073-8{_}353{_}Thus Dickens again and again has Mr. Micawber express a commonplace idea in sounding terms which at length fail him, so that he must interject an "in short" and summarize his meaning in a phrase amusing through its homely contrast.
10073-8{_}354{_}But humor based on ponderous diction is too often wearisome.
10073-8{_}355{_}Better say simply "He died," or colloquially "He kicked the bucket," than "He propelled his pedal extremities with violence against the wooden pail which is customarily employed in the transportation of the aquatic fluid."
10073-8{_}356{_}EXERCISE - Wordiness I  Express these ideas in simpler language:  The temperature was excessive.
10073-8{_}357{_}The most youthful of his offspring was not remarkable for personal pulchritude.
10073-8{_}358{_}Henry Clay expressed a preference for being on the right side of public questions to occupying the position of President of the United States of America.
10073-8{_}359{_}He who passes at an accelerated pace may nevertheless be capable of perusing.
10073-8{_}360{_}A masculine member of the human race was mounted on an equine quadruped.
10073-8{_}361{_}But the number of the terms we employ, as well as their ostentatiousness, must be considered.
10073-8{_}362{_}Most of us blunder around in the neighborhood of our meaning instead of expressing it briefly and clearly.
10073-8{_}363{_}We throw a handful of words at an idea when one word would suffice; we try to bring the idea down with a shotgun instead of a rifle.
10073-8{_}364{_}Of course one means of correction is that we should acquire accuracy, a quality already discussed.
10073-8{_}365{_}Another is that we should practice condensation.
10073-8{_}366{_}First, let us learn to omit the words which add nothing to the meaning.
10073-8{_}367{_}Thus in the sentence "An important essential in cashing a check is that you should indorse it on the back," several words or groups of words needlessly repeat ideas which are expressed elsewhere.
10073-8{_}368{_}The sentence is as complete in substance, and far terser in form, when it reads "An essential in cashing a check is that you should indorse it."
10073-8{_}369{_}Next, let us, when we may, reduce phrases and even clauses to a word.
10073-8{_}370{_}Thus the clause at the beginning and the phrase at the close of the following sentence constitute sheer verbiage: "Men who have let their temper get the better of them are often in a mood to do harm to somebody."
10073-8{_}371{_}The sentence tells us nothing that may not be told in five words: "Angry men are often dangerous."
10073-8{_}372{_}Finally, let us substitute phrases or clauses for unnecessary sentences.
10073-8{_}373{_}The following series of independent assertions contains avoidable repetitions: "One morning I was riding on the subway to my work.
10073-8{_}374{_}It was always my custom to ride to my work on the subway.
10073-8{_}375{_}This morning I met Harry Blake."
10073-8{_}376{_}The full thought may better be embodied in a single sentence: "One morning, while I was, as usual, riding on the subway to my work, I met Harry Blake."
10073-8{_}377{_}By applying these instructions to any page at hand--one from your own writing, one from a letter some friend has sent you, one from a book or magazine--you will often be able to strike out many of the words without at all impairing the meaning.
10073-8{_}378{_}Another means of acquiring succinct expression is to practice the composition of telegrams and cable messages.
10073-8{_}379{_}You will of course lessen the cost by eliminating every word that can possibly be spared.
10073-8{_}380{_}On the other hand, you must bear it in mind that your punctuation will not be transmitted, and that the recipient must be absolutely safeguarded against reading together words meant to be separated or separating words meant to be read together.
10073-8{_}381{_}That is, your message must be both concise and unmistakably clear.

10073-8{_}405{_}Base a telegraphic night letter of not more than fifty words upon these circumstances:  (a) You have been sent to buy, if possible and as cheaply as possible, a majority of the stock in a given company.
10073-8{_}406{_}You find that many of the stockholders distrust or dislike the president and are willing to sell.
10073-8{_}407{_}Some of these ask only $50 a share for their holdings; the owners of 100 shares want as much as $92; the average price asked is $76.
10073-8{_}408{_}By buying out all the president's enemies, which you can now do beyond question, you would secure a bare majority of the stock.
10073-8{_}409{_}But $92 a share seems to you excessive; that is, you think that by working quietly among the president's friends you can get 100 shares at $77 or thereabouts and thus save approximately $1500.
10073-8{_}410{_}On the other hand, should your dealings with the friends of the president give him premature warning, he might stop the sales by these friends and himself begin buying from his enemies, and thus make your purchase of a majority of the stock impossible.
10073-8{_}411{_}Is the $1500 you would save worth the risk you would be obliged to take?
10073-8{_}412{_}You call for instructions.
10073-8{_}413{_}(b) You are telegraphing a metropolitan paper the results of a Congressional election.
10073-8{_}414{_}Philput, the Republican candidate, leads in the cities, from which returns are now complete.
10073-8{_}415{_}Wilkins, the Democratic candidate, leads in the country, from only certain districts of which-- those nearest the cities--returns have been heard.
10073-8{_}416{_}If the present proportionate division of the rural vote is maintained for the total, Philput will be elected by a plurality of three hundred votes.
10073-8{_}417{_}Philput asserts that the proportions will hold.
10073-8{_}418{_}Wilkins points out, however, that he is relatively stronger in the more remote districts and predicts that he will have a plurality of seven hundred votes.
10073-8{_}419{_}Smallbridge, an independent candidate, is apparently making a better race in the country than in the city, but he is so weak in both places that the ballots cast for him can scarcely affect the outcome unless the margin of victory is infinitesimal.

10073-8{_}430{_}A final device for escaping wordiness you will have discovered for yourself while composing telegrams and telegraphic night letters.
10073-8{_}431{_}It is to pass over details not vital to your purpose.
10073-8{_}432{_}Of course you must have due regard for circumstances; details needed for one purpose may be superfluous for another.
10073-8{_}433{_}But all of us are familiar with the person who loses her ideas in a rigmarole of prosaic and irrelevant facts.
10073-8{_}434{_}Such a person is Shakespeare's scatter-brained Dame Quickly.
10073-8{_}435{_}On one occasion this voluble woman is shrilly reproaching Sir John Falstaff for his indebtedness to her.
10073-8{_}436{_}"What is the gross sum that I owe thee?"
10073-8{_}437{_}he inquires.
10073-8{_}438{_}She might answer simply: "If thou wert an honest man, thyself and the money too.
10073-8{_}439{_}Thou didst promise to marry me.
10073-8{_}440{_}Deny it if thou canst."
10073-8{_}441{_}Instead, she plunges into a prolix recital of the circumstances of the engagement, so that the all-important fact that the engagement exists has no special emphasis in her welter of words.
10073-8{_}442{_}"If thou wert an honest man," she cries, "thyself and the money too.
10073-8{_}443{_}Thou didst swear to me upon a parcel-gilt goblet, sitting in my Dolphin-chamber, at the round table, by a sea-coal fire, upon Wednesday in Wheeson week, when the prince broke thy head for liking his father to a singing-man of Windsor, thou didst swear to me then, as I was washing thy wound, to marry me and make me my lady thy wife.
10073-8{_}444{_}Canst thou deny it?
10073-8{_}445{_}Did not goodwife Keech, the butcher's wife, come in then and call me gossip Quickly?
10073-8{_}446{_}coming in to borrow a mess of vinegar; telling us she had a good dish of prawns; whereby thou didst desire to eat some, whereby I told thee they were ill for a green wound?
10073-8{_}447{_}And didst thou not, when she was gone down stairs, desire me to be no more so familiarity with such poor people; saying that ere long they should call me madam?
10073-8{_}448{_}And didst thou not kiss me and bid me fetch thee thirty shillings?
10073-8{_}449{_}I put thee now to thy book-oath; deny it if thou canst."
10073-8{_}450{_}EXERCISE - Wordiness III  1.
10073-8{_}451{_}Study the following paragraph, decide which ideas are important, and strike out the details that merely clog the thought:  As I stepped into the room, I heard the clock ticking and that caused me to look at it.
10073-8{_}452{_}It sits on the mantelpiece with some layers of paper under one corner where the mantel is warped.
10073-8{_}453{_}When the papers slip out or we move the clock a little as we're dusting, the ticking stops right away.
10073-8{_}454{_}Of course the clock's not a new one at all, but it's an old one.
10073-8{_}455{_}It has been in the family for many a long year, yes, from even before my father's time.
10073-8{_}456{_}Let me see, it was bought by my grandfather.
10073-8{_}457{_}No, it couldn't have been grandfather that bought it; it was his brother.
10073-8{_}458{_}Oh, yes, I remember now; my mother told me all about it, and I'd forgotten what she said till this minute.
10073-8{_}459{_}But really my grandfather's brother didn't exactly buy it.
10073-8{_}460{_}He just traded for it.
10073-8{_}461{_}He gave two pigs and a saddle, that's what my mother said.
10073-8{_}462{_}You see, he was afraid his hogs might take cholera and so he wanted to get rid of them; and as for the saddle, he had sold his riding-horse and he didn't have any more use for that.
10073-8{_}463{_}Well, it isn't a valuable clock, like a grandfather clock or anything of that sort, though it is antique.
10073-8{_}464{_}As I was saying, when I glanced at it, it read seven minutes to six.
10073-8{_}465{_}I remember the time very well, for just then the factory whistle blew and I remember saying to myself: "It's seven minutes slow today."
10073-8{_}466{_}You see, it's old and we don't keep it oiled, and so it's always losing time.
10073-8{_}467{_}Hardly a day passes but I set it up--sometimes twice a day, as for the matter of that--and I usually go by the factory whistle too, though now and then I go by Dwight's gold watch.
10073-8{_}468{_}Well, anyhow, that tells me what time it was.
10073-8{_}469{_}I'm certain I can't be wrong.
10073-8{_}470{_}2.
10073-8{_}471{_}Study, on the other hand, The Castaway (Appendix 5) for its judicious use of details.
10073-8{_}472{_}Defoe in his stories is a supreme master of verisimilitude (likeness to truth).
10073-8{_}473{_}As we read him, we cannot help believing that these things actually happened.
10073-8{_}474{_}More than in anything else the secret of his lifelikeness lies in his constant faithfulness to reality.
10073-8{_}475{_}He puts in the little mishaps that would have befallen a man so situated, the things he would have done, the difficulties he might have avoided had he exercised forethought.
10073-8{_}476{_}Though Defoe had little insight into the complexities of man's inner life, he has not been surpassed in his accumulations of naturalistic outer details.
10073-8{_}477{_}These do not cumber his narrative; they contribute to its purpose and add to its effectiveness.
10073-8{_}478{_}In this selection (Appendix 5) observe how plausible are such homely details as Crusoe's seeing no sign of his comrades "except three of their hats, one cap, and two shoes that were not fellows"; as his difficulty in getting aboard the ship again; and as his having his clothes washed away by the rising of the tide.
10073-8{_}479{_}Find half a dozen other such incidents that You consider especially effective.
10073-8{_}480{_}<Verbal Discords>  We may pitch our talk or our writing in almost any I key we choose.
10073-8{_}481{_}Our mood may be dreamy or eager or hilarious or grim or blustering or somber or bantering or scornful or satirical or whatever we will.
10073-8{_}482{_}But once we have established the tone, we should not--except sometimes for broadly humorous effects--change it needlessly or without clear forewarning.
10073-8{_}483{_}If we do, we create a one or the other of two obstacles, or both of them, for whoever is trying to follow what we say.
10073-8{_}484{_}In the first place, we obscure our meaning.
10073-8{_}485{_}For example, we have; been speaking ironically and suddenly swerve into serious utterance; or we have been speaking seriously and then incongruously adopt an ironic tone.
10073-8{_}486{_}How are our listeners, our readers to take us?
10073-8{_}487{_}They are puzzled; they do not know.
10073-8{_}488{_}In the second place, we offend--perhaps in insidious, indefinable fashion--the esthetic proprieties; we violate the natural fitness of things.
10073-8{_}489{_}For example, we have been speaking with colloquial freedom, sprinkling our discourse with _shouldn't_ and _won't;_ suddenly we be come formal and say _should not_ and will _not_.
10073-8{_}490{_}Our meaning is as obvious as before, but the verbal harmony has been interrupted; our hearers or readers are uneasily aware of a break in the unity of tone.
10073-8{_}491{_}A speaker or writer is a host to verbal guests.
10073-8{_}492{_}When he invites them to his assembly, he gives each the tacit assurance that it will not be brought into fellowship with those which in one or another of a dozen subtle ways will be uncongenial company for it.
10073-8{_}493{_}He must never be forgetful of this unspoken promise.
10073-8{_}494{_}If he is to avoid a linguistic breach, he must constantly have his wits about him; must study out his combinations carefully, and use all his knowledge, all his tact.
10073-8{_}495{_}He will make due use of spontaneous impulse; but that this may be wise and disciplined, he will form the habit of curiosity about words, their stations, their savor, their aptitudes, their limitations, their outspokenness, their reticences, their affinities and antipathies.
10073-8{_}496{_}Thus when he has need of a phrase to fill out a verbal dinner party, he will know which one to select.
10073-8{_}497{_}Certain broad classifications of words are manifest even to the most obtuse user of English.
10073-8{_}498{_}_Shady, behead_, and _lying_ are "popular" words, while their synonyms _umbrageous_ decapitate,_ and _mendacious_ are "learned" words.
10073-8{_}499{_}_Flabbergasted_ and _higgledy-piggledy_ are "colloquial," while _roseate_ and _whilom_ are "literary."
10073-8{_}500{_}_Affidavit, allegro_, _lee shore_, and _pinch hit_ are "technical," while _vamp_, _savvy, bum hunch_, and _skiddoo_ are "slang."
10073-8{_}501{_}It would be disenchanting indeed were extremes of this sort brought together.
10073-8{_}502{_}But offenses of a less glaring kind are as hard to shut out as February cold from a heated house.
10073-8{_}503{_}Unusual are the speeches or compositions, even the short ones, in which every word is in keeping, is in perfect tune with the rest.
10073-8{_}504{_}For the attainment of this ultimate verbal decorum we should have to possess knowledge almost unbounded, together with unerring artistic instinct.
10073-8{_}505{_}But diction of a kind only measurably inferior to this is possible to us if we are in earnest.
10073-8{_}506{_}To attain it we must study the difference between abstract and concrete terms, and let neither intrude unadvisedly upon the presence or functions of the other; do the same by literal and figurative terms and instruct ourselves in the nature and significance of connotation.
10073-8{_}507{_}Before considering these more detailed matters, however, we may pause for a general exercise on verbal harmony.
10073-8{_}508{_}EXERCISE - Discords  1.

10073-8{_}518{_}In the following passages, make such changes and omissions as are necessary to unify the tone:  How I loved to stroll, on those long Indian summer afternoons, into the quiet meadows where the mild-breathed kine were grazing!
10073-8{_}519{_}An old cow that switches her tail at flies and puts her foot in the bucket when you milk her, I absolutely loathe.
10073-8{_}520{_}How I loved to hear the birds sing, to listen to the fall of ripe autumnal apples!

10073-8{_}525{_}<I.
10073-8{_}526{_}Abstract vs.
10073-8{_}527{_}Concrete Terms; General vs.
10073-8{_}528{_}Specific Terms>  Abstract terms convey ideas; concrete terms call up pictures.
10073-8{_}529{_}If we say "Honesty is the best policy," we speak abstractly.
10073-8{_}530{_}Nobody can see or hear or touch the thing _honesty_ or the thing _policy_; the apprehension of them must be purely intellectual.
10073-8{_}531{_}But if we say "The rat began to gnaw the rope," we speak concretely.
10073-8{_}532{_}_Rat_, _gnaw_, and _rope_ are tangible, perceptible things; the words bring to us visions of particular objects and actions.
10073-8{_}533{_}Now when we engage in explanations and discussions of principles, theories, broad social topics, and the like--when we expound, moralize, or philosophize,--our subject matter is general.
10073-8{_}534{_}We approach our readers or hearers on the thinking, the rational side of their natures.
10073-8{_}535{_}Our phraseology is therefore normally abstract.
10073-8{_}536{_}But when, on the other hand, we narrate an event or depict an appearance, our subject matter is specific.
10073-8{_}537{_}We approach our readers or hearers on the sensory or emotional side of their natures.
10073-8{_}538{_}Our phraseology is therefore normally concrete.
10073-8{_}539{_}You should be able to express yourself according to either method.
10073-8{_}540{_}You should be able to choose the words best suited to make people understand; also to choose the words best suited to make people realize vividly and feel.
10073-8{_}541{_}Now to some extent you will adopt the right method by intuition.
10073-8{_}542{_}But if you do not reinforce your intuition with a careful study of words, you will vacillate from one method to the other and strike crude discords of phrasing.
10073-8{_}543{_}Of course if you switch methods intelligently and of purpose, that is quite another matter.
10073-8{_}544{_}An abstract discussion may be enlivened by a concrete illustration.
10073-8{_}545{_}A concrete narrative or portrayal may be given weight and rationalized by generalization.
10073-8{_}546{_}Moreover many things lie on the borderland between the two domains and may properly be attached to either.
10073-8{_}547{_}Thus the abstraction is legitimate when you say or write: "A man wishes to acquire the comforts and luxuries, as well as the necessaries, of life."
10073-8{_}548{_}The concreteness is likewise legitimate when you say or write: "John Smith wishes to earn cake as well as bread and butter."
10073-8{_}549{_}In most instances general terms are the same as abstract, and specific the same as concrete.
10073-8{_}550{_}Some subtle discriminations may, however, be made.
10073-8{_}551{_}Of these the only one that need concern us here is that the wording of a passage may not be abstract and yet be general.
10073-8{_}552{_}Suppose, for example, you were telling the story of the prodigal son and should say: "He was very hungry, and could; not obtain food anywhere.
10073-8{_}553{_}When he had come to his senses, he thought, 'I should be better off at home.'"
10073-8{_}554{_}This language is not abstract, but it is general rather than specific.
10073-8{_}555{_}When Jesus told the story, he wished to put the situation as poignantly as possible and therefore avoided both abstract and general terms: "And he would fain have filled his belly with the husks that the swine did eat: and no man gave unto him.
10073-8{_}556{_}And when he came to himself, he said, How many hired servants of my father's have bread enough and to spare, and I perish with hunger!"
10073-8{_}557{_}Many a person who shuns abstractions and talks altogether of the concrete things of life, yet traps out circumstance in general rather than specific terms.
10073-8{_}558{_}To do this is always to sacrifice force.

10073-8{_}625{_}<2.
10073-8{_}626{_}Literal vs. Figurative Terms>  Phraseology is literal when it says exactly what it means; is figurative when it says one thing, but really means another.
10073-8{_}627{_}Thus "He fought bravely" is literal; "He was a lion in the fight" is figurative.
10073-8{_}628{_}Literal phraseology as a rule appeals to our scientific or understanding faculties; figurative to our emotional faculties.
10073-8{_}629{_}Here again, as with abstraction and concreteness, you should learn to express yourself by either method.
10073-8{_}630{_}Both have their advantages and their drawbacks.
10073-8{_}631{_}We all admire the man who has observed, and can state, accurately.
10073-8{_}632{_}It is upon this belief of ours in the literal that Defoe shrewdly traffics.
10073-8{_}633{_}(See Appendix 5.)
10073-8{_}634{_}He does not stir us as some writers do, but he gains our implicit confidence.
10073-8{_}635{_}Dame Quickly, on the contrary, makes egregious use of the literal.
10073-8{_}636{_}(See paragraph above EXERCISE - Wordiness III above.)
10073-8{_}637{_}Her facts are accurate, yes; but how strictly, how unsparingly accurate!
10073-8{_}638{_}And how many of them are beside the point!
10073-8{_}639{_}She quite convinces us that the devotee of the literal may be dull.
10073-8{_}640{_}An advantage of the figurative also is that it may make meanings lucid.
10073-8{_}641{_}Thus when Burke near the close of his discussion (Appendix 2) wishes to make it clear that by a law of nature the authority of extensive empires is slighter in its more remote territories, he has recourse to a figure of speech: "In large bodies, the circulation of power must be less vigorous at the extremities.
10073-8{_}642{_}Nature has said it."
10073-8{_}643{_}More often, however, the function of the figurative is to drive home a thought or a mood of which a mere statement would leave us unmoved--to make us _feel_ it.
10073-8{_}644{_}Thus Burke said of the Americans "Their love of liberty, as with you, fixed and attached on this specific point of taxing."
10073-8{_}645{_}He added: "Here they felt its pulse, and as they found that beat they thought themselves sick or sound."
10073-8{_}646{_}Had you been one of his Parliamentary hearers, would not that second sentence have made more real and more important the colonial attitude to taxation?
10073-8{_}647{_}The poets of course make frequent and noble use of the figurative.
10073-8{_}648{_}This is how Coleridge tells us that the descent of a tropical night is sudden:    "The sun's rim dips; the stars rush out;   At one stride comes the dark."
10073-8{_}649{_}The words _rush out_ and _at one stride comes_ convert the stars and the darkness into vast beings or at least vast personal forces; the comparisons are so natural as to seem inevitable; we are transported to the very scene and feel the overwhelming abruptness of the nightfall.
10073-8{_}650{_}But if a figure of speech seems artificial, if it is strained or far-fetched or merely decorative, it subtracts from the effectiveness of the passage.
10073-8{_}651{_}Thus when Tennyson says:    "When the breeze of a joyful dawn blew free   In the silken sail of infancy."
10073-8{_}652{_}we must stop and ponder before we perceive that what he means is "When I was a happy child."
10073-8{_}653{_}The figure is like an exotic plant rather than a natural outgrowth of the soil; it appears to us something thought up and stuck on; it is a parasite rather than a helper.
10073-8{_}654{_}Of course, as with abstraction and concreteness, you should develop facility in gliding from literalness to figurativeness and back again.
10073-8{_}655{_}But you are always to remember that your gymnastics are not to militate against verbal concord.
10073-8{_}656{_}You must never set words scowling and growling at each other through injudicious combinations like this: "She was five feet, four and three-quarter inches high, had a small, round scar between her nose and her left cheek-bone, and moved with the lissom and radiant grace of a queen."

10073-8{_}709{_}She glanced at the mirror, but did not really see herself.
10073-8{_}710{_}She was trying to puzzle out the right course, and could only see as through a glass darkly.
10073-8{_}711{_}Arming himself with the sword of zeal and the buckler of integrity, he wrote the letter.
10073-8{_}712{_}He swept the floor every morning, and was a ray of sunshine in the office.
10073-8{_}713{_}He also emptied the waste baskets and cleaned the cuspidors.
10073-8{_}714{_}<3.
10073-8{_}715{_}Connotation>  The connotation of a word is the subtle implication, the emotional association it carries--often quite apart from its dictionary definition.
10073-8{_}716{_}Thus the words _house_ and _home_ in large measure overlap in meaning, but emotionally they are not equivalents at all.
10073-8{_}717{_}You can say _house_ without experiencing any sensation whatever, but if you utter the word _home_ it will call back, however slightly, tender and cherished recollections.
10073-8{_}718{_}_Bald heads_ and _gray hair_ are both indicative of age; but you would pronounce the former in disparaging allusion to elderly persons, and the latter with sentiments of veneration.
10073-8{_}719{_}You would say, of a clodpole that he plays the _fiddle_, but of Fritz Kreisler that he plays the _violin_.
10073-8{_}720{_}And just as you unconsciously adapt words to feelings in these obvious instances, you must learn, on peril of striking false notes verbally, to do so when distinctions are less gross.
10073-8{_}721{_}Moreover circumstance as well as sentiment may control the connotation of a word.
10073-8{_}722{_}A word or phrase may have a double or triple connotation, and depend upon vocal inflection, upon gesture, upon the words with which it is linked, upon the experience of speaker or hearer, upon time, place, and external fact, or upon other forces outside it for the sense in which it is to be taken.
10073-8{_}723{_}You may be called "old dog" in an insulting manner, or (especially if a slap on the shoulder accompanies the phrase) in an affectionate manner.
10073-8{_}724{_}You may properly say, "Calhoun had logic on his side"; add, however, the words "but his face was to the past," and you spoil the sentence,--for _face_ gives a reflex connotation to _side_, slight perhaps and momentary, but disconcerting.
10073-8{_}725{_}Think over the funny stories you have heard.
10073-8{_}726{_}Many of them turn, you will find, on the outcropping of new significance in a phrase because of its environment.
10073-8{_}727{_}Thus the anecdote of the servant who had been instructed to summon the visiting English nobleman by tapping on his bedroom door and inquiring, "My lord, have you yet risen?"
10073-8{_}728{_}and who could only stammer, "My God!
10073-8{_}729{_}ain't you up yet?"
10073-8{_}730{_}Or the anecdote of the minister who in a sermon on the Parable of the Prodigal Son told how a young man living dissolutely in a city had been compelled to send to the pawnbroker first his overcoat, next his suit, next his silk shirt, and finally his very underclothing--"and then," added the minister, "he came to himself."
10073-8{_}731{_}Only by unresting vigilance can you evade verbal discords, if not of this magnitude, at least of much frequency and stylistic harm.
10073-8{_}732{_}EXERCISE - Connotation  1.
10073-8{_}733{_}Note the contrast in emotional suggestion that comes to you from hearing the words:  "Sodium chloride" and "salt" "A test-tube of H2O" and "a cup of cold water" "A pair of brogans" and "a little empty shoe" "Bump" and "collide" "A brilliant fellow" and "a flashy fellow" "Bungled it" and "did not succeed" "Tumble" and "fall" "Dawn" and "6 A.M." "Licked" and "worsted" "Fat" and "plump" "Wept" and "blubbered" "Cheek" and "self-assurance" "Stinks" and "disagreeable odors" "Steal" and "embezzle" "Thievishness" and "kleptomania" "Educated" and "highbrow" "Job" and "Position" "Told a lie" and "fell into verbal inexactitude" "A drunkard" (a stranger) and "a drunkard" (your father).
10073-8{_}734{_}2.

10073-8{_}761{_}Try.
10073-8{_}762{_}The music, yearning like a god in pain.
10073-8{_}763{_}Alone, alone, all, all alone,     Alone on a wide, wide sea!
10073-8{_}764{_}But O for the touch of a vanished hand,   And the sound of a voice that is still!
10073-8{_}765{_}Old, unhappy, far-off things,     And battles long ago.
10073-8{_}766{_}It was night in the lonesome October.
10073-8{_}767{_}How they tinkle, tinkle, tinkle,     In the icy air of night!
10073-8{_}768{_}While the stars, that oversprinkle     All the heavens, seem to twinkle     With a crystalline delight.
10073-8{_}769{_}The moan of doves in immemorial elms,   And murmuring of innumerable bees.

10073-8{_}771{_}Merrily, merrily, shall I live now,   Under the blossom that hangs on the bough.
10073-8{_}772{_}'Tis as easy now for the heart to be true   As for grass to be green or skies to be blue,--   'Tis the natural way of living.
