10073-8{_}1456{_}V.      INDIVIDUAL WORDS: AS MEMBERS OF VERBAL FAMILIES   Our investigation into the nature, qualities, and fortunes of single words must now merge into a study of their family connections.
10073-8{_}1457{_}We do not go far into this new phase of our researches before we perceive that the career of a word may be very complicated.
10073-8{_}1458{_}Most people, if you asked them, would tell you that an individual word is a causeless entity--a thing that was never begotten and lacks power to propagate.
10073-8{_}1459{_}They would deny the possibility that its course through the world could be other than colorless, humdrum.
10073-8{_}1460{_}Now words thus immaculately conceived and fatefully impotent, words that shamble thus listlessly through life, there are.
10073-8{_}1461{_}But many words are born in an entirely normal way; have a grubby boyhood, a vigorous youth, and a sober maturity; marry, beget sons and daughters, become old, enfeebled, even senile; and suffer neglect, if not death.
10073-8{_}1462{_}In their advanced age they are exempted by the discerning from enterprises that call for a lusty agility, but are drafted into service by those to whom all levies are alike.
10073-8{_}1463{_}Indeed in their very prime of manhood their vicissitudes are such as to make them seem human.
10073-8{_}1464{_}Some rise in the world some sink; some start along the road of grandeur or obliquity, and then backslide or reform.
10073-8{_}1465{_}Some are social climbers, and mingle in company where verbal dress coats are worn; some are social degenerates, and consort with the ragamuffins and guttersnipes of language.
10073-8{_}1466{_}Some marry at their own social level, some above them, some beneath; some go down in childless bachelorhood or leave an unkempt and illegitimate progeny.
10073-8{_}1467{_}And if you trace their own lineage, you will find for some that it is but decent and middle-class, for some that it is mongrelized and miscegenetic, for some that it is proud, ancient, yea perhaps patriarchal.
10073-8{_}1468{_}It is contrary to nature for a word, as for a man, to live the life of a hermit.
10073-8{_}1469{_}Through external compulsion or internal characteristics a word has contacts with its fellows.
10073-8{_}1470{_}And its most intimate, most spontaneous associations are normally with its own kindred.
10073-8{_}1471{_}In our work hitherto we have had nothing to say of verbal consanguinity.
10073-8{_}1472{_}But we have not wholly ignored its existence, for the very good reason that we could not.
10073-8{_}1473{_}For example, in the latter portions of Chapter IV we proceeded on the hypothesis that at least some words have ancestors.
10073-8{_}1474{_}Also in the analysis of the dictionary definition of _tension_ we learned that the word has, not only a Latin forebear, but French, Spanish, Portuguese, and Italian kinsmen as well.
10073-8{_}1475{_}One thing omitted from that analysis would have revealed something further--namely, that the word has its English kinfolks too.
10073-8{_}1476{_}For the bracketed part of the dictionary definition mentions two other English words, _tend_ and _tense_, which from their origin involve the same idea as that of _tension_-- the idea of stretching.
10073-8{_}1477{_}Now words may be akin in either of two ways.
10073-8{_}1478{_}They may be related in blood.
10073-8{_}1479{_}Or they may be related by marriage.
10073-8{_}1480{_}Let us consider these two kinds of connection more fully.
10073-8{_}1481{_}<Words Related in Blood>  As an illustration of blood kinships enjoyed by a native English word take the adjective _good_.
10073-8{_}1482{_}We can easily call to mind other members of its family: goodly, goodish, goody-goody, good-hearted, good-natured, good- humored, good-tempered, goods, goodness, goodliness, gospel (good story), goodby, goodwill, goodman, goodwife, good-for-nothing, good den (good evening), the Good Book.
10073-8{_}1483{_}The connection between these words is obvious.
10073-8{_}1484{_}Next consider a group of words that have been naturalized: scribe, prescribe, ascribe, proscribe, transcribe, circumscribe, subscriber, indescribable, scribble, script, scripture, postscript, conscript, rescript, manuscript, nondescript, inscription, superscription, description.
10073-8{_}1485{_}It is clear that these words are each other's kith and kin in blood, and that the strain or stock common to all is _scribe_ or (as sometimes modified) _script_.
10073-8{_}1486{_}What does this strain signify?
10073-8{_}1487{_}The idea of writing.
10073-8{_}1488{_}The _scribes_ are a writing clan.
10073-8{_}1489{_}Some of them, to be sure, have strayed somewhat from the ancestral calling, for words are as wilful--or as independent--as men.
10073-8{_}1490{_}_Ascribe_, for example, does not act like a member of the household of writers, whatever it may look like.
10073-8{_}1491{_}We should have to scrutinize it carefully or consult the record for it in that verbal Who's Who, the dictionary, before we could understand how it came by its scribal affiliations honestly.
10073-8{_}1492{_}But once we begin to reflect or to probe, we find we have not mistaken its identity.
10073-8{_}1493{_}_Ascribe_ is the offspring of _ad_ (to) and _scribo_ (write), both Latin terms.
10073-8{_}1494{_}It originally meant writing to a person's name or after it (that is, imputing to the person by means of written words) some quality or happening of which he was regarded as the embodiment, source, or cause.
10073-8{_}1495{_}Nowadays we may saddle the matter on him through oral rather than written speech.
10073-8{_}1496{_}That is, _ascribe_ has largely lost the writing traits.
10073-8{_}1497{_}But all the same it is manifestly of the writing blood.
10073-8{_}1498{_}The _scribes_ are of undivided racial stock, Latin.
10073-8{_}1499{_}Consider now the _manu_, or _man_, words which sprang from the Latin _manus_, meaning "hand."
10073-8{_}1500{_}Here are some of them: manual, manoeuver, mandate, manacle, manicure, manciple, emancipate, manage, manner, manipulate, manufacture, manumission, manuscript, amanuensis.
10073-8{_}1501{_}These too are children of the same father; they are brothers and sisters to each other.
10073-8{_}1502{_}But what shall we say of legerdemain (light, or sleight, of hand), maintain, coup de main, and the like?
10073-8{_}1503{_}They bear a resemblance to the _man's_ and _manu's_, yet one that casual observers would not notice.
10073-8{_}1504{_}Is there kinship between the two sets of words?
10073-8{_}1505{_}There is.
10073-8{_}1506{_}But not the full fraternal or sororal relation.
10073-8{_}1507{_}The _mains_ are children of _manus_ by a French marriage he contracted.
10073-8{_}1508{_}With this French blood in their veins, they are only half-brothers, half-sisters of the _manu's_ and the _man's_.
10073-8{_}1509{_}Your examination of the family trees of words will be practical, rather than highly scholastic, in nature.
10073-8{_}1510{_}You need not track every word in the dictionary to the den of its remote parentage.
10073-8{_}1511{_}Nor need you bother your head with the name of the distant ancestor.
10073-8{_}1512{_}But in the case of the large number of words that have a numerous kindred you should learn to detect the inherited strain.
10073-8{_}1513{_}You will then know that the word is the brother or cousin of certain other words of your acquaintance, and this knowledge will apprise you of qualities in it with which you should reckon.
10073-8{_}1514{_}To this extent only must you make yourself a student of verbal genealogy.
10073-8{_}1515{_}EXERCISE - Blood  (Simple exercises in tracing blood relationships among words are given at the end of the chapter.
10073-8{_}1516{_}Therefore the exercises assigned here are of a special character.)
10073-8{_}1517{_}1.
10073-8{_}1518{_}Each of the following groups is made up of related words, but the relationship is somewhat disguised.
10073-8{_}1519{_}Consult the dictionary for each word, and learn all you can as to (a) its source, (b) the influence (as passing through an intermediate language) that gave it its present form, (c) the course of its development into its present meaning.
10073-8{_}1520{_}Captain                 Cathedral         Governor Capital                 Chaise            Gubernatorial Decapitate              Chair Chef                    Shay              Guardian Chieftain                                 Ward                         Camp Cavalry                 Campaign          Guarantee Chivalry                Champion          Warrant  Camera                  Inept             Incipient Chamber                 Apt               Receive  Serrated                Inimical          Poor Sierra                  Enemy             Pauper  Influence               Espionage         Work Influenza               Spy               Wrought                                           Playwright Isolate Insular  2.
10073-8{_}1521{_}The variety of sources for modern English is indicated by the following list.
10073-8{_}1522{_}Do not seek for blood kinsmen of these particular words, but think of all the additional words you can that have come into English from Indian, Spanish, French, any other language spoken today.
10073-8{_}1523{_}Alphabet (Greek)              Piano (Italian) Folio (Latin)                 Car (Norman) Boudoir (French)              Rush (German) Binnacle (Portuguese)         Sky (Icelandic) Anger (Old Norse)             Yacht (Dutch) Isinglass (Low German)        Hussar (Hungarian) Slogan (Celtic)               Samovar (Russian) Polka (Polish)                Chess (Persian) Shekel (Hebrew)               Tea (Chinese) Algebra (Arabic)              Kimono (Japanese) Puttee (Hindoo)               Tattoo (Tahitian) Boomerang (Australian)        Voodoo (African) Potato (Haytian)              Skunk (American Indian) Guano (Peruvian)              Buncombe (American) Renegade (Spanish)   <Words Related by Marriage>  That words marry and are given in marriage, is too generally overlooked.
10073-8{_}1524{_}Any student of a foreign language, German for instance, can recall the thrill of discovery and the lift of reawakened hope that came to him when first he suspected, aye perceived, the existence of verbal matrimony.
10073-8{_}1525{_}For weeks he had struggled with words that apparently were made up of fortuitous collocations of letters.
10073-8{_}1526{_}Then in some beatific moment these huddles of letters took meaning; in instance after instance they represented, not a word, but words--a linguistic household.
10073-8{_}1527{_}Let them be what they might--a harem, the domestic establishment of a Mormon, the dwelling-place of verbal polygamists,--he could at last see order in their relationships.
10073-8{_}1528{_}To their morals he was indifferent, absorbed as he was in his joy of understanding.
10073-8{_}1529{_}In English likewise are thousands of these verbal marriages.
10073-8{_}1530{_}We may not be aware of them; from our very familiarity with words we may overlook the fact that in instances uncounted their oneness has been welded by a linguistic minister or justice of the peace.
10073-8{_}1531{_}But to read a single page or harken for thirty seconds to oral discourse with our minds intent on such states of wedlock is to convince ourselves that they abound.
10073-8{_}1532{_}Consider this list of everyday words: somebody, already, disease, vineyard, unskilled, outlet, nevertheless, holiday, insane, resell, schoolboy, helpmate, uphold, withstand, rainfall, deadlock, typewrite, football, motorman, thoroughfare, snowflake, buttercup, landlord, overturn.
10073-8{_}1533{_}Every term except one yokes a verbal husband with his wife, and the one exception (_nevertheless_) joins a uxorious man with two wives.
10073-8{_}1534{_}These marriages are of a simple kind.
10073-8{_}1535{_}But the nuptial interlinkings between families of words may be many and complicated.
10073-8{_}1536{_}Thus there is a family of _graph_ (or write) words: graphic, lithograph, cerograph, cinematograph, stylograph, telegraph, multigraph, seismograph, dictograph, monograph, holograph, logograph, digraph, autograph, paragraph, stenographer, photographer, biographer, lexicographer, bibliography, typography, pyrography, orthography, chirography, calligraphy, cosmography, geography.
10073-8{_}1537{_}There is also a family of _phone_ (or sound) words: telephone, dictaphone, megaphone, audiphone, phonology, symphony, antiphony, euphonious, cacophonous, phonetic spelling.
10073-8{_}1538{_}It chances that both families are of Greek extraction.
10073-8{_}1539{_}Related to the _graphs_--their cousins in fact--are the _grams_: telegram, radiogram, cryptogram, anagram, monogram, diagram, logogram, program, epigram, kilogram, ungrammatical.
10073-8{_}1540{_}Now a representative of the _graphs_ married into the _phone_ family, and we have graphophone.
10073-8{_}1541{_}A representative of the _phones_ married into the _graph_ family, and we have phonograph.
10073-8{_}1542{_}A representative of the _grams_ married into the _phone_ family, and we have gramophone.
10073-8{_}1543{_}A representative of the _phones_ married into the _gram_ family, and we have phonogram.
10073-8{_}1544{_}Of such unions children may be born.
10073-8{_}1545{_}For example, from the marriage of Mr.
10073-8{_}1546{_}Phone with Miss Graph were born phonography, phonographer, phonographist (a rather frail child), phonographic, phonographical, and phonographically.
10073-8{_}1547{_}Intermarriage between the _phones_ and the _graphs_ or _grams_ is a wedding of equals.
10073-8{_}1548{_}Some families of words, however, are of inferior social standing to other families, and may seek but not hope to be sought in marriage.
10073-8{_}1549{_}Compare the _ex's_ with the _ports_.
10073-8{_}1550{_}An _ex_, as a preposition, belongs to a prolific family but not one of established and unimpeachable dignity.
10073-8{_}1551{_}Hence the _ex's_, though they marry right and left, lead the other words to the altar and are never led thither themselves.
10073-8{_}1552{_}Witness exclude, excommunicate, excrescence, excursion, exhale, exit, expel, expunge, expense, extirpate, extract; in no instance does _ex_ fellow its connubial mate--it invariably precedes.
10073-8{_}1553{_}The _ports_, on the other hand, are the peers of anybody.
10073-8{_}1554{_}Some of them choose to remain single: port, porch, portal, portly, porter, portage.
10073-8{_}1555{_}Here and there one marries into another family: portfolio, portmanteau, portable, port arms.
10073-8{_}1556{_}More often, however, they are wooed than themselves do the pleading: comport, purport, report, disport, transport, passport, deportment, importance, opportunity, importunate, inopportune, insupportable.
10073-8{_}1557{_}From our knowledge of the two families, therefore, we should surmise that if any marriage is to take place between them; an _ex_ must be the suitor.
10073-8{_}1558{_}The surmise would be sound.
10073-8{_}1559{_}There is such a term as _export_, but not as _portex_.
10073-8{_}1560{_}Now it is oftentimes possible to do business with a man without knowing whether he is a man or a bridal couple.
10073-8{_}1561{_}And so with a word.
10073-8{_}1562{_}But the knowledge of his domestic state and circumstances will not come amiss, and it may prove invaluable.
10073-8{_}1563{_}You may find that you can handle him to best advantage through a sagacious use of the influence of his wife.
10073-8{_}1564{_}EXERCISE - Marriage  1.
10073-8{_}1565{_}For each word in the lists of EXERCISE - Dictionary and Activity 1 for EXERCISE - Past, determine (a) whether it is single or married; (b) if it is married, whether the wedding is one between equals.
10073-8{_}1566{_}2.
10073-8{_}1567{_}Make a list of the married words in the first three paragraphs of the selection from Burke (Appendix 2).
10073-8{_}1568{_}For each of these words determine the exact nature and extent of the dowry brought by each of the contracting parties to the wedding.
10073-8{_}1569{_}<Prying Into a Word's Relationships>  Hitherto in our study of verbal relationships we have usually started with the family.
10073-8{_}1570{_}Having strayed (as by good luck) into an assembly of kinsmen, we have observed the common strain and the general characteristics, and have then "placed" the individual with reference to these.
10073-8{_}1571{_}But we do not normally meet words, any more than we meet men, in the domestic circle.
10073-8{_}1572{_}We meet them and greet them hastily as they hurry through the tasks of the day, with no other associates about them than such as chance or momentary need may dictate.
10073-8{_}1573{_}If we are to see anything of their family life, it must be through effort we ourselves put forth.
10073-8{_}1574{_}We must be inquisitive about their conjugal and blood relationships.
10073-8{_}1575{_}How, then, starting with the individual word, can you come into a knowledge of it, not in its public capacity, but in what is even more important, its personal connections?
10073-8{_}1576{_}You must form the habit of asking two questions about it: (1) Is it married?
10073-8{_}1577{_}(2) Of what family or families was it born?
10073-8{_}1578{_}If you can get an understanding answer to these two questions, an answer that will tell you what its relations stand for as well as what their name is, your inquiries will be anything but bootless.
10073-8{_}1579{_}Let us illustrate your procedure concretely.
10073-8{_}1580{_}Suppose you read or hear the word _conchology_.
10073-8{_}1581{_}It is a somewhat unusual word, but see what you can do with it yourself before calling on the dictionary to help you.
10073-8{_}1582{_}Observe the word closely, and you will obtain the answer to your first question.
10073-8{_}1583{_}_Conchology_ is no bachelor, no verbal old maid; it is a married pair.
10073-8{_}1584{_}Your second and more difficult task awaits you; you must ascertain the meaning of the family connections.
10073-8{_}1585{_}With Mr. Conch you are on speaking terms; you know him as one of the shells.
10073-8{_}1586{_}But the utmost you can recall about his wife is that she is one of a whole flock of _ologies_.
10073-8{_}1587{_}What significance does this relationship possess?
10073-8{_}1588{_}You are uncertain.
10073-8{_}1589{_}But do not thumb the dictionary yet.
10073-8{_}1590{_}Pass in mental review all the _ologies_ you can assemble.
10073-8{_}1591{_}Wait also for the others that through the unconscious operations of memory will tardily straggle in.
10073-8{_}1592{_}Be on the lookout for _ologies_ as you read, as you listen.
10073-8{_}1593{_}In time you will muster a sizable company of them.
10073-8{_}1594{_}And you will draw a conclusion as to the meaning of the blood that flows through their veins.
10073-8{_}1595{_}_Ology_ implies speech or study.
10073-8{_}1596{_}_Conchology_, then, must be the study of conches.
10073-8{_}1597{_}Your investigations thus far have done more than teach you the meaning of the word you began with.
10073-8{_}1598{_}They have brought you some of the by-products of the study of verbal kinships.
10073-8{_}1599{_}For you no longer pass the _ologies_ by with face averted or bow timidly ventured.
10073-8{_}1600{_}You have become so well acquainted with them that even a new one, wherever encountered, would flash upon you the face of a friend.
10073-8{_}1601{_}But now your desires are whetted.
10073-8{_}1602{_}You wish to find out how much you _can_ learn.
10073-8{_}1603{_}You at last consult the dictionary.
10073-8{_}1604{_}Here a huge obstacle confronts you.
10073-8{_}1605{_}The _ologies_, like the _ports_ (above), are a haughty clan; they are the wooed, rather than the wooing, members of most marital households that contain them.
10073-8{_}1606{_}Now the marriage licenses recorded in the dictionary are entered under the name of the suitor, not of the person sought.
10073-8{_}1607{_}Hence you labor under a severe handicap as you take the census of the _ologies_.
10073-8{_}1608{_}Let us imagine the handicap the most severe possible.
10073-8{_}1609{_}Let us suppose that no _ology_ had ever been the suitor.
10073-8{_}1610{_}Even so, you would not be entirely baffled.
10073-8{_}1611{_}For you could look up in the dictionary the _ologies_ you your self had been able to recall.
10073-8{_}1612{_}To what profit?
10073-8{_}1613{_}First, you could verify or correct your surmise as to what the _ological_ blood betokens.
10073-8{_}1614{_}Secondly, you could perhaps obtain cross-references to yet other _ologies_ than those you remembered.
10073-8{_}1615{_}But you are not reduced to these extremities.
10073-8{_}1616{_}The _ologies_, arrogant as they are, sometimes are the applicants for matrimony, and the marriage registry of the dictionary so indicates.
10073-8{_}1617{_}To be sure, they do not, when thus appearing at the beginning of words, take the form _ology_.
10073-8{_}1618{_}They take the form _log_.
10073-8{_}1619{_}But you must be resourceful enough to keep after your quarry in spite of the omission of a vowel or two.
10073-8{_}1620{_}Also from some lexicons you may obtain still further help.
10073-8{_}1621{_}You may find _ology, logy, logo_, or _log_ listed as a combining form, its meaning given, and examples of its use in compounds cited.
10073-8{_}1622{_}By your zeal and persistence you have now brought together a goodly array of the _ologies_--all or most, let us say, of the following: conchology, biology, morphology, phrenology, physiology, osteology, histology, zoology, entomology, bacteriology, ornithology, pathology, psychology, cosmology, eschatology, demonology, mythology, theology, astrology, archeology, geology, meteorology, mineralogy, chronology, genealogy, ethnology, anthropology, criminology, technology, doxology, anthology, trilogy, philology, etymology, terminology, neologism, phraseology, tautology, analogy, eulogy, apology, apologue, eclogue, monologue, dialogue, prologue, epilogue, decalogue, catalogue, travelogue, logogram, logograph, logo-type, logarithms, logic, illogical.
10073-8{_}1623{_}(Moreover you may have perceived in some of these words the kinship which exists in all for the _loquy_ group--see (1) Soliloquy below.)
10073-8{_}1624{_}Of course you will discard some items from this list as being too learned for your purposes.
10073-8{_}1625{_}But you will observe of the others that once you know the meaning of _ology_, you are likely to know the whole word.
10073-8{_}1626{_}Thus from your study of _conchology_ you have mastered, not an individual term, but a tribe.
10073-8{_}1627{_}In _conchology_ only one element, _ology_, was really dubious at the outset.
10073-8{_}1628{_}Let us take a word of which both elements give you pause.
10073-8{_}1629{_}Suppose your thought is arrested by the word _eugenics_.
10073-8{_}1630{_}You perhaps know the word as a whole, but not its components.
10073-8{_}1631{_}For by looking at it and thinking about it you decide that its state is married, that it comprises the household of Mr. Eu and his wife, formerly Miss Gen.
10073-8{_}1632{_}But you cannot say offhand just what kind of person either Mr. Eu or the erstwhile Miss Gen is likely to prove.
10073-8{_}1633{_}Have you met any of the _Eu's_ elsewhere?
10073-8{_}1634{_}You think vaguely that you have, but cannot lay claim to any real acquaintance.
10073-8{_}1635{_}To the dictionary you accordingly betake yourself.
10073-8{_}1636{_}There you find that Mr. Eu is of a family quite respectable but not prone to marriage.
10073-8{_}1637{_}_Euphony, eupepsia, euphemism, euthanasia_ are of his retiring kindred.
10073-8{_}1638{_}The meaning of the _eu_ blood, so the dictionary informs you, is well.
10073-8{_}1639{_}The _gen_ blood, as you see exemplified in gentle, general, genital, engender, carries with it the idea of begetting, of producing, of birth, or (by extension) of kinship.
10073-8{_}1640{_}_Eugenics_, then, is an alliance of well and begotten (or born).
10073-8{_}1641{_}Your immediate purpose is fulfilled; but you resolve, let us say, to make the acquaintance of more of the _gens_, whose number you have perceived to be legion.
10073-8{_}1642{_}You are duly introduced to the following: genus, generic, genre, gender, genitive, genius, general, Gentile, gentle, gentry, gentleman, genteel, generous, genuine, genial, congeniality, congener, genital, congenital, engender, generation, progeny, progenitor, genesis, genetics, eugenics, pathogenesis, biogenesis, ethnogeny, palingenesis, unregenerate, degenerate, monogeny, indigenous, exogenous, homogeneous, heterogeneous, genealogy, ingenuous, ingenious, ingenue, engine, engineer, hygiene, hydrogen, oxygen, endogen, primogeniture, philoprogeniture, miscegenation.
10073-8{_}1643{_}Some of these are professional rather than social; you decide not to leave your card at their doors.
10073-8{_}1644{_}Others have assumed a significance somewhat un_gen_-like, though the relationship may be traced if you are not averse to trouble, Thus _engine_ in its superficial aspects seems alien to the idea of born.
10073-8{_}1645{_}But it is the child of _ingenious_ (innate, inborn); _ingenious_ is the inborn power to accomplish, and _engine_ is the result of the application of that power.
10073-8{_}1646{_}Whether you care to bother with such subtleties or not, enough _gens_ are left to make the family one well worth your cultivation.
10073-8{_}1647{_}Thus by studying two words, _conchology_ and _eugenics_, you have for the first time placed yourself on an intimate footing with three verbal families--the _ologies_, the _eu's,_ and the _gens_.
10073-8{_}1648{_}Observe that though you studied the _ologies_ apart from the _eu's_ and the _gens_, your knowledge--once you have acquired it--cannot be kept pigeonholed, for the _ologies_ have intermarried with both the other families.
10073-8{_}1649{_}Hence you on meeting _eulogy_ can exclaim: "How do you do, Mr. Eu?
10073-8{_}1650{_}I am honored in making your acquaintance, Mrs. Eu--I was about to call you by your maiden name; for I am a friend of your sister, the Miss Ology who married Mr. Conch.
10073-8{_}1651{_}And you too, Mr. Eu--I cannot regard you as a stranger.
10073-8{_}1652{_}I have looked in so often on the family of your brother--the Euphony family, I mean.
10073-8{_}1653{_}What a beautiful literary household it is!
10073-8{_}1654{_}Yet it has been neglected by the world-yea, even by the people who write.
10073-8{_}1655{_}Well, the loss is theirs who do the neglecting."
10073-8{_}1656{_}And _genealogy_ you can greet with an equal parade of family lore: "Don't trouble to tell me who you are.
10073-8{_}1657{_}I am hob and nob with your folks on both sides of the family, and my word for it, the relationship is written all over you.
10073-8{_}1658{_}Mr. Gen, I envy you the pride you must feel in the prominence given nowadays to the _eugenics_ household.
10073-8{_}1659{_}And it must delight you, Miss Ology-that-was, that connoisseurs are so keenly interested in _conchology_.
10073-8{_}1660{_}How are Grandfather Gen and Grandmother Ology?
10073-8{_}1661{_}They were keeping up remarkably the last time I saw them."
10073-8{_}1662{_}Do you think words will not respond to cordiality like this?
10073-8{_}1663{_}They will work their flattered heads off for you!
10073-8{_}1664{_}EXERCISE - Relationships  1.
10073-8{_}1665{_}For each of the following words (a) determine what families are intermarried, (b) ascertain the exact contribution to the household by each family represented, and (c) make as complete a list as possible of cognate words.
10073-8{_}1666{_}Reject     Oppose     Convent     Defer     Omit     Produce     Expel  2.
10073-8{_}1667{_}Test the extent of the intermarriages among these words by successively attaching each of the prefixes to each of the main (or key) syllables.
10073-8{_}1668{_}(Thus re-ject, re-fer, re-pel, etc.)
10073-8{_}1669{_}<Two Admonitions>  In tracing verbal kinships you must be prepared for slight variations in the form of the same key-syllable.
10073-8{_}1670{_}Consider these words: wise, wiseacre, wisdom, wizard, witch, wit, unwitting, to wit, outwit, twit, witticism, witness, evidence, providence, invidious, advice, vision, visit, vista, visage, visualize, envisage, invisible, vis-√†-vis, visor, revise, supervise, improvise, proviso, provision, view, review, survey, vie, envy, clairvoyance.
10073-8{_}1671{_}Perhaps the last six should be disregarded as too exceptional in form to be clearly recognized.
10073-8{_}1672{_}And certainly some words, as _prudence_ from _providentia_, are so metamorphosed that they should be excluded from practical lists of this kind.
10073-8{_}1673{_}But even in the words left to us there are fairly marked divergences in appearance.
10073-8{_}1674{_}Why?
10073-8{_}1675{_}Because the key-syllable has descended to us, not through one language, but through several.
10073-8{_}1676{_}As good verbal detectives we should be able to penetrate the consequent disguises; for _wis, wiz, wit, vid, vic_, and _vis_ all embody the idea of seeing or knowing.
10073-8{_}1677{_}On the other hand, you must take care not to be misled by a superficial resemblance into thinking two unrelated key-syllables identical.
10073-8{_}1678{_}Let us consider two sets of words.
10073-8{_}1679{_}The first, which is related to the _tain_ group (see <Tain> below), has a key-syllable that means holding: tenant, tenement, tenure, tenet, tenor, tenable, tenacious, contents, contentment, lieutenant, maintenance, sustenance, countenance, appurtenance, detention, retentive, pertinacity, pertinent, continent, abstinence, continuous, retinue.
10073-8{_}1680{_}The second has a key-syllable that means stretching: tend, tender, tendon, tendril, tendency, extend, subtend, distend, pretend, contend, attendant, tense, tension, pretence, intense, intensive, ostensible, tent, tenterhook, portent, attention, intention, tenuous, attenuate, extenuate, antenna, tone, tonic, standard.
10073-8{_}1681{_}The form of the key-syllable for the first set of words is usually _ten, tent_, or _tin_; that for the second _tend, tens, tent_, or _ten_.
10073-8{_}1682{_}You may therefore easily confuse the two groups until you have learned to look past appearances into meanings.
10073-8{_}1683{_}Thenceforth the holdings and the stretchings will be distinct in your mind--will constitute two great families, not one.
10073-8{_}1684{_}Of course individual words may still puzzle you.
10073-8{_}1685{_}You will not perceive that _tender_, for example, belongs with the stretchings until you go back to its primary idea of something stretched thin, or that _tone_ has membership in that family until you connect it with the sound which a stretched chord emits.
10073-8{_}1686{_}FIRST GENERAL EXERCISE FOR THE CHAPTER  Each of the key-syllables given below is followed by (1) a list of fairly familiar words that embody it, (2) a list of less familiar words that embody it, (3) several sentences containing blank spaces, into each of which you are ultimately to fit the appropriate word from the first list.
10073-8{_}1687{_}(The existence of the two lists will show you that learned words may have commonplace kinfolks.)
10073-8{_}1688{_}First, however, you are to study each word in both lists for (1) its exact meaning, (2) the influence of the key-syllable upon that meaning, (3) any variation of the key-syllable from its ordinary form.
10073-8{_}1689{_}(A few words have been introduced to show how varied the forms may be and yet remain recognizable.)
10073-8{_}1690{_}Also, as an aid to your memory, you are to copy each list, underscoring the key-syllable each time you encounter it.
10073-8{_}1691{_}(The lists are practical, not meticulously academic.
10073-8{_}1692{_}In many instances they contain words derived, not from a single original, but from cognates.
10073-8{_}1693{_}No list is exhaustive.)

10073-8{_}2314{_}How does it affect the meaning of the word?
10073-8{_}2315{_}Does it appear, perhaps in disguised form, in any of the words immediately preceding or following?
10073-8{_}2316{_}Can you bring to mind other words that embody it?

10073-8{_}2318{_}Witness the _Good_ family and the _Stead_ family.
10073-8{_}2319{_}The families in which kinship may be overlooked are likely to be of Latin or Greek ancestry, though perhaps with a subsequent infusion of blood from some other foreign language, as French.
10073-8{_}2320{_}Hitherto our approach to verbal families has been through the descendants, or through that quality in their blood which holds them together.
10073-8{_}2321{_}But we shall also profit from knowing something of the founders of these families--from having some acquaintance with them as individuals.
10073-8{_}2322{_}Below (in separate lists) the more prominent of Latin and of Greek progenitors are named, their meaning is given, and two or three of their living representatives (not always direct descendants) are designated.
10073-8{_}2323{_}Starred [*] words are those whose progeny has not been in good part assembled in the preceding pages; for these words you should assemble all the living representatives you can.
10073-8{_}2324{_}(Inflectional forms are given only where they are needed for tracing English derivatives.)

    epidemic, epithet, epode, ephemeral *Hyper          over, extremely      hypercritical, hyperbola *Hypo           under, in smaller    hypodermic, hypophosphate                   measure *Meta           after, over          metaphysics, metaphor *Para           beside               paraphrase, paraphernalia *Peri           around, about        periscope, peristyle *Pro            before               proboscis, prophet *Syn            together, with       synthesis, synopsis, sympathy    VI      WORDS IN PAIRS   Our first task in this volume was the study of words in combination.
10073-8{_}2326{_}Our second was the study of individual words in two of their aspects--first, as they are seen in isolation, next as they are seen in verbal families.
10073-8{_}2327{_}Now our third task confronts us.
10073-8{_}2328{_}It is the study of words as they are associated, not in actual blood kinship, but in meaning.
10073-8{_}2329{_}Such an association in meaning may involve only two words (pairs) or larger groups.
10073-8{_}2330{_}In this chapter we shall confine ourselves to the study of pairs.
10073-8{_}2331{_}Of the relationship between pairs there are three types.
10073-8{_}2332{_}In the first the words are hostile to each other.
10073-8{_}2333{_}In the second they may easily be confused with each other.
10073-8{_}2334{_}In the third they are parallel with each other.
10073-8{_}2335{_}We shall examine the three types successively.
10073-8{_}2336{_}But we must make an explanation first.
10073-8{_}2337{_}Although we shall, in this and the following chapters, have frequent occasion to give the meanings of individual words, we shall give them without regard to dictionary methods.
10073-8{_}2338{_}We shall not attempt formal, water-tight, or exhaustive definitions; our purpose is to convey, in the simplest and most human manner possible, brief general explanations of what the words stand for.
10073-8{_}2339{_}<Opposites>  Pairs of the first type are made up of words by nature opposite to each other, or else thought of as opposite because they are so often contrasted.
10073-8{_}2340{_}Here is a familiar, everyday list:  east, west       straight, crooked       myself, others large, small     pretty, ugly            major, minor laugh, cry       walk, ride              light, darkness top, bottom      hard, soft              friend, enemy sweet, sour      clean, dirty            temporal, spiritual meat, drink      merry, sad              means, extremes land, water      private, public         Jew, Gentile man, woman       noisy, quiet            independent, dependent old, new         general, particular     sublime, ridiculous age, youth       wholesale, retail       give, receive sick, well       savage, civilized       pride, humility brain, brawn     wealth, poverty         constructive, destructive soul, body       positive, negative  None of these words needs explaining.
10073-8{_}2341{_}If you think of one of them, you will think of its opposite; at least its opposite will be lurking in the back of your mind.
10073-8{_}2342{_}As proof of this fact you have only to glance at the following list, from which the second member of each pair is omitted:  hot--         black--       boy--        in-- off--         over--        love--       wrong-- strong--      wet--         first--      day-- long--        fast--        good--       hope-- least--       asleep--      buy--        left-- alive--       winter--      war--        succeed-- creditor--    fat--         internal--   wise-- drunk--  Many words of a more difficult kind are thus pitted against each other, and we learn them, not singly, but in pairs.
10073-8{_}2343{_}At least we should.
10073-8{_}2344{_}As good verbal hunters we should be alert to the chance of killing two birds with one stone.
10073-8{_}2345{_}_Allopath_ and _homeopath_, for example, are difficult opposites.
10073-8{_}2346{_}We know of the existence of the two classes of medical practitioners; we know that they use different methods; but beyond this our knowledge is likely to be hazy.
10073-8{_}2347{_}Let us set out, then, to _learn_ the two words.
10073-8{_}2348{_}The best way is to learn them together.
10073-8{_}2349{_}_Allopathy_ means other suffering, _homeopathy_ like suffering.
10073-8{_}2350{_}An allopath uses remedies which create within the patient a condition that squarely conflicts with the further progress of the disease.
10073-8{_}2351{_}A homeopath prescribes medicines (in small doses) which produce within the patient the same condition that the disease would produce; he "beats the disease to it," so to speak--takes the job himself and leaves the disease nothing to do.
10073-8{_}2352{_}The allopath travels around a race-track in the opposite direction from the disease, and thwarts it through a head-on collision.
10073-8{_}2353{_}The homeopath travels around the race-track in the same direction as the disease, and thwarts it by pulling at the reins.
10073-8{_}2354{_}If we consider the two words together and get these ideas in mind, we shall have no further trouble with allopaths and homeopaths--except, perhaps, when they have rendered their services and presented their bills.
10073-8{_}2355{_}_Objective_ and _subjective_ are also a troublesome pair.
10073-8{_}2356{_}A thing is objective if it is an actual object or being, if it exists in itself rather than in our surmises.
10073-8{_}2357{_}A thing is subjective if it is the creature of a state of mind, if it has its existence in the thought or imagination of some person or other.
10073-8{_}2358{_}Thus if I meet a bear in the wilds, that bear is objective; whatever may be the state of my thoughts, _he is there_--and it would be to my advantage to reckon with this fact.
10073-8{_}2359{_}But if a child who is sent off to bed alone says there is a bear in the room, the bear is subjective; it is not a living monster that will devour anybody, but a creature called into the mind of the child through dread.
10073-8{_}2360{_}EXERCISE - Opposites  Study the following words in pairs.
10073-8{_}2361{_}Consult the dictionary for actual meanings.
10073-8{_}2362{_}Then test your knowledge by embodying each word of each pair in a sentence, or in an illustration like those of the race-track and the bear in the preceding paragraphs.
10073-8{_}2363{_}superior, inferior           concord, discord export, import               domestic, foreign fact, fiction                prose, poetry verbal, oral                 literal, figurative predecessor, successor       genuine, artificial positive, negative           practical, theoretical optimism, pessimism          finite, infinite longitude, latitude          evolution, revolution oriental, occidental         pathos, bathos sacred, profane              military, civil clergy, laity                capital, labor ingress, egress              element, compound horizontal, perpendicular    competition, co√∂peration predestination, freewill     universal, particular extrinsic, intrinsic         inflation, deflation dorsal, ventral              acid, alkali synonym, antonym             prologue, epilogue nadir, zenith                amateur, connoisseur anterior, posterior          stoic, epicure ordinal, cardinal            centripetal, centrifugal stalagmite, stalactite       orthodox, heterodox homogeneous, heterogeneous   monogamy, polygamy induction, deduction         egoism, altruism Unitarian, Trinitarian       concentric, eccentric herbivorous, carnivorous     deciduous, perennial esoteric, exoteric           endogen, exogen vertebrate, invertebrate     catalectic, acatalectic   <Words Often Confused>  Pairs of the second type are made up of words which are often confused by careless writers and speakers, and which should be accurately discriminated.
10073-8{_}2364{_}Sometimes the words are actually akin to each other.
10073-8{_}2365{_}_Continuous- continual_ and _enormity-enormousness_ are examples.
10073-8{_}2366{_}Sometimes they merely look or sound much alike.
10073-8{_}2367{_}_Mean-demean_ and _affect- effect_ are examples.
10073-8{_}2368{_}Sometimes the things they designate are more or less related, so that the ideas behind the words rather than the words themselves are responsible for the confusion.
10073-8{_}2369{_}_Contagious-infectious_ and _knowledge-wisdom_ are examples.
10073-8{_}2370{_}Let us distinguish between the two members of each of the pairs named.
10073-8{_}2371{_}A thing is _continuous_ if it suffers no interruption whatever, _continual_ if it is broken at regular intervals but as regularly renewed.
10073-8{_}2372{_}Thus "a continuous stretch of forest"; "the continual drip of water from the eaves."
10073-8{_}2373{_}_Enormity_ pertains to the moral and sometimes the social, _enormousness_ to the physical.
10073-8{_}2374{_}Thus "the enormity of the crime," "the enormity of this social offense"; "the enormousness of prehistoric animals."
10073-8{_}2375{_}_Demean_ is often used reproachfully because of its supposed relation to _mean_.
10073-8{_}2376{_}But it has nothing to do with _mean_.
10073-8{_}2377{_}The word with which to connect it is _demeanor_ (conduct).
10073-8{_}2378{_}Thus "We observed how he demeaned himself" implies no adverse criticism of either the man or his deportment.
10073-8{_}2379{_}Both may be debased to be sure, but they may be exemplary.
10073-8{_}2380{_}To _affect_ means to feign or to have an influence upon, to _effect_ to bring to pass.
10073-8{_}2381{_}Thus "He affects a fondness for classical music," "The little orphan's story affected those who heard it"; "We effected a compromise."
10073-8{_}2382{_}_Affect_ is never properly used as a noun.
10073-8{_}2383{_}_Effect_ as a noun means result, consequence, or practical operation.
10073-8{_}2384{_}Thus "The shot took instant effect"; "He put this idea into effect."
10073-8{_}2385{_}A disease is _contagious_ when the only way to catch it is through direct contact with a person already having it, or through contact with articles such a person has used.
10073-8{_}2386{_}A disease is _infectious_ when it is presumably caused, not by contact with a person, but through widespread general conditions, as of climate or sanitation.
10073-8{_}2387{_}Our _knowledge_ is our acquaintance with a fact, or the sum total of our information.
10073-8{_}2388{_}Our _wisdom_ is our intellectual and spiritual discernment, to which our knowledge is one of the contributors.
10073-8{_}2389{_}_Knowledge_ comprises the materials; _wisdom_ the ability to use them to practical advantage and to worthy or noble purpose.
10073-8{_}2390{_}_Knowledge_ is mental possession; _wisdom_ is mental and moral power.

10073-8{_}2512{_}<Compulsion, obligation.> Who does not feel within him a compulsion to help the weak?
10073-8{_}2513{_}It was through obligation, through having slave-drivers stand over them, that these wretched folk built the pyramids.
10073-8{_}2514{_}<Congratulate, felicitate.> I congratulated my friend on his appointment to the commission.
10073-8{_}2515{_}I also felicitated the stranger on his appointment.
10073-8{_}2516{_}<Consecutive, successive.> Three consecutive convictions proved the ability of the prosecuting attorney.
10073-8{_}2517{_}The quiet passing of successive summer days.
10073-8{_}2518{_}<Contemptible, contemptuous.> Its size was insignificant, even contemptible.
10073-8{_}2519{_}He won the prize by a contemptuous trick.
10073-8{_}2520{_}<Continuation, continuance.> The investigator was surprised to find the tradition of such long continuation.
10073-8{_}2521{_}We waited impatiently for the continuance of the story in the next issue.
10073-8{_}2522{_}<Corporal, corporeal.> I am more and more amazed at the perfection of man's corporal frame.
10073-8{_}2523{_}His corporeal vigor was unusual.
10073-8{_}2524{_}<Correct, rectify.> A man may correct many of his false judgments on current affairs by studying history.
10073-8{_}2525{_}The mistake is ours; it shall be rectified.
10073-8{_}2526{_}<Cozy, snug.> The cozy fit of a garment.
10073-8{_}2527{_}A snug place by the fire.
10073-8{_}2528{_}<Crawl, creep.> We crawled forward at dawn to surprise their outposts.
10073-8{_}2529{_}In his humility he fairly crept on the earth.
10073-8{_}2530{_}<Credible, creditable.> I do not doubt it; it is entirely credible.
10073-8{_}2531{_}The success of the antidote seemed scarcely creditable.
10073-8{_}2532{_}<Credit, accredit.> Though he is the official and credited ambassador, his assertions are not accredited.
10073-8{_}2533{_}<Cure, heal.> I cured the dog's wounds.
10073-8{_}2534{_}The physician declared he could heal leprosy.
10073-8{_}2535{_}<Custom, habit.> "A custom more honor'd in the breach than the observance."
10073-8{_}2536{_}Is it your custom to watch the clock while you eat?
10073-8{_}2537{_}The habit in that region was to rise at cockcrow.
10073-8{_}2538{_}<Decided, decisive.> A decided battle.
10073-8{_}2539{_}A decisive fault in manners.
10073-8{_}2540{_}<Definite, definitive.> We still await a definite edition of this author's works.
10073-8{_}2541{_}His answer was so definitive that we no longer doubted what he meant.
10073-8{_}2542{_}<Demesne, domain.> Clive added India to the British demesne.
10073-8{_}2543{_}The king went riding through his personal domain.
10073-8{_}2544{_}<Deprecate, depreciate.> The German mark has deprecated in value.
10073-8{_}2545{_}He depreciated the praise they were lavishing upon him.
10073-8{_}2546{_}<Descent, dissent.> They tied themselves together with a rope in order to make their dissent safer.
10073-8{_}2547{_}The dissent to a lower plane of conversation was what he most desired.
10073-8{_}2548{_}<Discovery, invention.> The discovery of the wireless telegraph is Marconi's chief claim to remembrance.
10073-8{_}2549{_}The invention of a water passage between Tierra del Fuego and the mainland was the work of Magellan.
10073-8{_}2550{_}<Discriminate, distinguish.> He could not discriminate individuals at that distance.
10073-8{_}2551{_}Any man can distinguish right from wrong.
10073-8{_}2552{_}<Disinterested, uninterested.> His course was entirely generous and disinterested.
10073-8{_}2553{_}Most visitors to art galleries have an uninterested manner.
10073-8{_}2554{_}<Disposal, disposition.> This disposal of the matter is authoritative, final.
10073-8{_}2555{_}His disposition of his forces was well-considered.
10073-8{_}2556{_}<Dissatisfied, discontented.> Though the colonists were dissatisfied for the moment, they could hardly be called discontented.
10073-8{_}2557{_}<Distinct, distinctive.> The distinct quality of his character was aggressiveness.
10073-8{_}2558{_}There were four separate and distinctive calls.
10073-8{_}2559{_}<Dramatic, theatrical.> An affected, dramatic manner.
10073-8{_}2560{_}A truly theatrical situation.
10073-8{_}2561{_}<Dry, arid.> A dry plain.
10073-8{_}2562{_}An arid place to sleep in.
10073-8{_}2563{_}<Dumb, mute.> The man stood dumb with surprise.
10073-8{_}2564{_}Always be kind to mute animals.
10073-8{_}2565{_}<Durable, lasting.> Our joy is durable.
10073-8{_}2566{_}Oak is a lasting wood.
10073-8{_}2567{_}3.
10073-8{_}2568{_}Consult the dictionary for the distinction between the members of each of the following pairs.
10073-8{_}2569{_}Frame sentences to illustrate the correct use of the words.
10073-8{_}2570{_}(Some of the words in this list, as well as some in other parts of the chapter, are considered in larger groups in the chapters following.)

           warn, caution will, volition              wit, humor witness, see                womanish, womanlike worth, value       <Parallels>  Pairs of the third type are made up of words parallel in meaning.
10073-8{_}2572{_}This class somewhat overlaps the second; many terms that are frequently confused are parallels, and parallelism is of course a cause of confusion.
10073-8{_}2573{_}Parallels are words that show likeness in meaning.
10073-8{_}2574{_}Likeness, not sameness.
10073-8{_}2575{_}Yet at one time actual sameness may have existed, and in many instances did.
10073-8{_}2576{_}Nowadays this sameness has been lost, and the words have become differentiated.
10073-8{_}2577{_}As a rule they still are closely related in thought; sometimes, however, the divergence between them is wide.
10073-8{_}2578{_}Why did words having the same meaning find lodgment in the language in the first place?
10073-8{_}2579{_}The law of linguistic economy forbids any such happening, and only through sheer good fortune did English come to possess duplications.
10073-8{_}2580{_}The original Anglo-Saxon did not contain them.
10073-8{_}2581{_}But the Roman Catholic clergy brought to England the language of religion and of scholarship, Latin.
10073-8{_}2582{_}Later the Normans, whose speech as a branch of French was an offshoot of Latin, came to the island as conquerors.
10073-8{_}2583{_}For a time, therefore, three languages existed side by side in the country--Anglo- Saxon among the common folk, Latin among the clergy, and Norman-French at the court and among the nobility.
10073-8{_}2584{_}The coalescing of the three (or of the two if we count Latin in its direct and indirect contributions as one) was inevitable.
10073-8{_}2585{_}But other (mostly cognate) languages also had a part in the speech that was ultimately evolved.
10073-8{_}2586{_}The Anglo-Saxon element was augmented by words from Dutch, Scandinavian, and the  Germanic tongues in general; and Latin was reinforced by Greek.
10073-8{_}2587{_}Thus to imply, as is sometimes done, that modern English is simply a blend of Anglo-Saxon and Latin elements is misleading.
10073-8{_}2588{_}_Native_ and _classic_ are the better terms to use, provided both are used broadly.
10073-8{_}2589{_}_Native_ must include not only Anglo-Saxon but the other Germanic elements as well, and _classic_ must include French and Greek as well as Latin.
10073-8{_}2590{_}The welding of these languages made available two--in some instances more than two--words for a single object or idea.
10073-8{_}2591{_}What became of these duplicates?
10073-8{_}2592{_}Sometimes one of the words was dropped as needless.
10073-8{_}2593{_}Oftentimes, however, both were retained--with such modifications in meaning that thereafter they designated, not the same object or idea, but different forms or aspects of it.
10073-8{_}2594{_}Thus they became parallels, and the new language waxed rich with discriminations which neither of the component tongues had possessed.
10073-8{_}2595{_}Scott in _Ivanhoe_ gives the basis upon which the unification of the languages proceeded.
10073-8{_}2596{_}The jester Wamba in conversation with the swineherd Gurth explains how the Anglo-Saxon term took on the homelier, rougher, more workaday uses and left the more refined and fastidious uses for the Norman-French.
10073-8{_}2597{_}A domestic animal, says Wamba, was cared for by the conquered people, and in consequence bore while living a "good Saxon" name--swine, ox, or calf; but it was served at the tables of the conquerors, and therefore when ready for consumption bore a "good Norman-French" name--pork, beef, or veal.
10073-8{_}2598{_}"When the brute [a sow] lives, and is in charge of a Saxon slave, she goes by her Saxon name; but becomes Norman and is called pork, when she is carried into the castle hall to feast among the nobles....
10073-8{_}2599{_}He [a calf] is Saxon when he requires tendance, and takes a Norman name [Monsieur de Veau] when he becomes matter of enjoyment."
10073-8{_}2600{_}Let us see how Scott's contention fares if we extend his list of terms relative to animal life.
10073-8{_}2601{_}As throughout the rest of this chapter, with the single and necessary exception of List B, the first word in each pair is native, the second classic:   <LIST A>  sheep, mutton          deer, venison        horse, equine cow, bovine            bull, taurine        sheep, ovine wolf, lupine           hog, porcine         bear, ursine fox, vulpine           cat, feline          dog, canine fish, piscatorial      mouse, vermin        rat, rodent mankind, humanity      man, masculine       woman, feminine childish, infantile    boyish, puerile  A glance at this list will show that, at least as regards animal life, the native word is likely to be the more familiar and unpretentious.
10073-8{_}2602{_}But we must not leap to the conclusion that, taking the language as a whole, the simple, easy word is sure to be native, the abstruse word classic.
10073-8{_}2603{_}In the following list one word in each pair is simpler, oftentimes much simpler, than the other; yet both are of classic origin.
10073-8{_}2604{_}(In some instances the two are doublets; that is, they spring from the same stem.)

               prudent, provident      pupil, disciple story, narrative          pause, interval         despise, abhor doctor, physician         fate, destiny           country, rustic aged, senile              increase, increment     gentle, genteel clear, apparent           eagle, aquiline         motion, momentum nourishment, nutrition    pure, unadulterated     closeness, proximity number, divide      hearten, encourage hoard, treasure   Again, the native word is sometimes less emphatic than the classic:  <LIST D>  fly, soar             old, venerable         flood, cataclysm steep, precipitous    wonder, astonishment   speed, velocity sparkle, scintillate  stir, commotion        stir, agitate strike, collide       learned, erudite       small, diminutive scare, terrify        burn, combustion       fire, conflagration fall, collapse        uproot, eradicate      skin, excoriate hate, abominate       work, labor            bright, brilliant hungry, famished      eat, devour            twisted, contorted thin, emaciated       sad, lugubrious        mirth, hilarity  Despite these exceptions, the native word is in general better known and more crudely powerful than the classic.
10073-8{_}2606{_}Thus of the pair _sweat-perspiration, sweat_ is the plain-spoken, everyday member, _perspiration_ the polite, even learned member.
10073-8{_}2607{_}The man of limited vocabulary says _sweat_; even the sophisticated person, unless there is occasion to soften effects, finds _sweat_ the more natural term.
10073-8{_}2608{_}No one would say that a horse perspires.
10073-8{_}2609{_}No one would say that human beings must eat their bread in the perspiration of their faces.
10073-8{_}2610{_}But _sweat_ is a word of connotation too vigorous (though honest withal) for us to use the term in the drawing room.
10073-8{_}2611{_}A questionable woman in _The Vicar of Wakefield_ betrays her lack of breeding by the remark that she is in a muck of sweat.
10073-8{_}2612{_}The native word, besides being in itself simpler and starker than the classic, makes stronger appeal to our feelings and affections.
10073-8{_}2613{_}In nearly every instance the objects and relationships that have woven themselves into the very texture of our lives are designated by native terms.
10073-8{_}2614{_}Even if they are not so designated solely, they are so designated in their more cherished aspects.
10073-8{_}2615{_}We warm more to the native _fatherly_ than to the classic _paternal_.
10073-8{_}2616{_}We have a deeper sentiment for the native _home_ than for the classic _residence_.
10073-8{_}2617{_}That the native is the more downright term may be seen from the following words.
10073-8{_}2618{_}(These pairs are of course merely illustrative.
10073-8{_}2619{_}With them might be grouped a few special pairs, like _devilish-diabolical_ and _church_-_ecclesiastical_, of which the first members are classic in origin but of such early naturalization into English that they may be regarded as native.)
10073-8{_}2620{_}<LIST E>  belly, stomach           belly, abdomen       navel, umbilicus suck, nurse              naked, nude          murder, homicide dead, deceased           dead, defunct        dying, moribund lust, salacity           lewd, libidinous     read, peruse lie, prevaricate         hearty, cordial      following, subsequent crowd, multitude         chew, masticate      food, pabulum eat, regale              meal, repast         meal, refection thrift, economy          sleepy, soporific    slumberous, somnolent live, reside             rot, putrefy         swelling, protuberant soak, saturate           soak, absorb         stinking, malodorous spit, saliva             spit, expectorate    thievishness, kleptomania belch, eructate          sticky, adhesive     house, domicile eye, optic               walker, pedestrian   talkative, loquacious talkative, garrulous     wisdom, sapience     bodily, corporeal name, appellation        finger, digit        show, ostentation nearness, propinquity    wash, lave           handwriting, chirography waves, undulations       shady, umbrageous    fat, corpulent muddy, turbid            widow, relict        horseback, equestrian weight, avoirdupois      blush, erubescence  The word of classic origin in many instances survives only or mainly in the form of an adjective; as a noun (or other part of speech) it has completely or largely disappeared.
10073-8{_}2621{_}This fact may be observed in lists already given, particularly List A.
10073-8{_}2622{_}It may also be observed in the following words:   <LIST F>  moon, lunar               star, stellar         star, sidereal sun, solar                earth, terrestrial    world, mundane heaven, celestial         hell, infernal        earthquake, seismic ear, aural                head, capital         hand, manual foot, pedal               breast, pectoral      heart, cardial hip, sciatic              tail, caudal          throat, guttural lung, pulmonary           bone, osseous         hair, hirsute tearful, lachrymose       early, primitive      sweet, dulcet, sweet, saccharine         young, juvenile       bloody, sanguinary deadly, mortal            red, florid           bank, riparian hard, arduous             wound, vulnerable     written, graphic spotless, immaculate      sell, mercenary       son, filial salt, saline              meal, farinaceous     wood, ligneous wood, sylvan              cloud, nebulous       glass, vitreous milk, lacteal             water, aquatic        stone, lapidary gold, aureous             silver, argent        iron, ferric honey, mellifluous        loving, amatory       loving, erotic loving, amiable           wedded, hymeneal      plow, arable priestly, sacerdotal      arrow, sagittal       wholesome, salubrious warlike, bellicose        timely, temporary     fiery, igneous ring, annular             soap, saponaceous     nestling, nidulant snore, stertorous         window, fenestral     twilight, crepuscular soot, fuliginous          hunter, venatorial  The fact that English is a double-barreled language, and that of parallel terms one is likely to be native and the other classic, is interesting in itself.
10073-8{_}2623{_}Our lists of parallels, however, though (with the exception of List B) they are arranged to bring out this duality of origin, have other and more vital uses as material for exercises.
10073-8{_}2624{_}For after all it matters little whether we know where a word comes from, provided we know thoroughly the meaning and implications of the word itself.
10073-8{_}2625{_}The lists already given and those to follow show the more important words actually yoked as parallels.
10073-8{_}2626{_}Your task must be to ascertain the differences in import between the words thus joined.
10073-8{_}2627{_}EXERCISE - Parallels  <LIST G>  Study the discriminations between the members of the following pairs.
10073-8{_}2628{_}At each blank in the illustrative sentences insert the appropriate word.
10073-8{_}2629{_}<Brotherly, fraternal.> _Brotherly_ is used of actual blood kinship, or indicates close feeling, deep affection, or religious love.
10073-8{_}2630{_}_Fraternal_ is used less personally and intimately; it normally betokens that the relations are at least in part formal (as relations within societies).
10073-8{_}2631{_}"The sight of the button on the stranger's lapel caused Wilkes to give him the cabalistic sign and ask his ____ assistance."
10073-8{_}2632{_}"Though the children of different parents, we bear for each other a true ____ devotion."
10073-8{_}2633{_}"Because we both are newspaper men I feel a ____ interest in him."
10073-8{_}2634{_}<Daily, diurnal.> _Daily_, the popular word, is often used loosely.
10073-8{_}2635{_}We may say that we eat three meals daily without implying that we have never gone dinnerless.
10073-8{_}2636{_}_Diurnal_, the scientific term, is used exactly, whether applying to the period of daylight or to the whole twenty-four hours.
10073-8{_}2637{_}A diurnal flower closes at night; a diurnal motion is precisely coincident with the astronomical day.
10073-8{_}2638{_}In poetry, however, _diurnal_ is often used for _daily_.
10073-8{_}2639{_}"Give us this day our ____ bread."
10073-8{_}2640{_}"The ____ rotation of the earth on its axis is the cause of our day and night."
10073-8{_}2641{_}"Fred and I went for our ____ ramble through the hills."
10073-8{_}2642{_}<Cold, frigid.> Which is the more popular word?
10073-8{_}2643{_}Let us see.
10073-8{_}2644{_}Would the man in the street be more likely to use one than the other?
10073-8{_}2645{_}Which one?
10073-8{_}2646{_}Does this answer our question?
10073-8{_}2647{_}Another question: Which word is the more inclusive in meaning?
10073-8{_}2648{_}Again, let us see.
10073-8{_}2649{_}A blacksmith is beating iron; does the iron grow cold or frigid?
10073-8{_}2650{_}Which term, then, approaches the closer in meaning to the idea of mere coolness?
10073-8{_}2651{_}On the other hand, may that same term represent a temperature far beyond mere coolness?
10073-8{_}2652{_}Would you speak of a morning as bitterly cold or bitterly frigid?
10073-8{_}2653{_}Now think of the term you have not been using.
10073-8{_}2654{_}_Can_ it convey as wide meanings, or is it limited in range?
10073-8{_}2655{_}Does the word _frigid_ carry for you a geographical suggestion (to the frigid zone)?
10073-8{_}2656{_}Do you yourself use the term?
10073-8{_}2657{_}If so, do you use it chiefly (perhaps entirely) in connection with human temperament or demeanor?
10073-8{_}2658{_}Is _cold_ used thus figuratively also?
10073-8{_}2659{_}Which is the more often thus used?
10073-8{_}2660{_}"I suffer from ____ hands and feet."
10073-8{_}2661{_}"The slopes of Mont Blanc are ____ with eternal snow."
10073-8{_}2662{_}"He did not warm to the idea at all.
10073-8{_}2663{_}His inclinations are absolutely ____."
10073-8{_}2664{_}<Manly, virile>.
10073-8{_}2665{_}_Manly_ implies possession of traits or qualities a man should possess; it may be used of immature persons.
10073-8{_}2666{_}_Virile_ implies maturity and robust masculinity; it is also used of the power to procreate.
10073-8{_}2667{_}"A ____ lad."
10073-8{_}2668{_}"A ____ reply."
10073-8{_}2669{_}"____ energy."
10073-8{_}2670{_}"____ and aggressive."
10073-8{_}2671{_}"____ forbearance,"  <Inner, internal>.
10073-8{_}2672{_}_Inner_ is somewhat within, or more within than something else is; it is also used in figurative and spiritual senses.
10073-8{_}2673{_}_Internal_ is entirely within.
10073-8{_}2674{_}"The ____ organs of the human body."
10073-8{_}2675{_}"The ____ layer of the rind."
10073-8{_}2676{_}"The injury was ____."
10073-8{_}2677{_}"The ____ nature of man."
10073-8{_}2678{_}"The ____ meaning of the occurrence."
10073-8{_}2679{_}<Height, altitude>.
10073-8{_}2680{_}"He was five feet, eleven inches in height."
10073-8{_}2681{_}Can you substitute _altitude_?
10073-8{_}2682{_}Is _altitude_ used of persons?
10073-8{_}2683{_}"At an altitude of eleven feet from the ground."
10073-8{_}2684{_}Would _height_ be more natural?
10073-8{_}2685{_}Does _altitude_ betoken great height?
10073-8{_}2686{_}If so, does Hamlet speak jestingly when he greets the player, "Your ladyship is nearer heaven than when I saw you last, by the altitude of a chopine?"
10073-8{_}2687{_}What of the sentence: "The altitude of Galveston was not sufficient to protect it from the tidal wave"?
10073-8{_}2688{_}Does the magnitude or importance of the object (Galveston) compensate for its lack of elevation and thus justify _altitude_?
10073-8{_}2689{_}Could _height_ be substituted?
10073-8{_}2690{_}If so, would the words _above sea-level_ have to follow it?
10073-8{_}2691{_}Does this fact give you a further clue as to the distinction between the two words?
10073-8{_}2692{_}You are comparing the elevation of two peaks, both plainly visible; you measure them merely by your eye.
10073-8{_}2693{_}Do you say "This exceeds the other in height" or "This exceeds the other in altitude"?
10073-8{_}2694{_}Suppose the peaks are so distant from each other that the two are not visible simultaneously, and suppose you are speaking from a knowledge of the scientific measurements.
10073-8{_}2695{_}Do you say "This exceeds the other in height" or "This exceeds the other in altitude"?
10073-8{_}2696{_}<Talk, conversation>.
10073-8{_}2697{_}_Talk_ may be one-sided and empty.
10073-8{_}2698{_}_Conversation_ requires that at least two shall participate, and it is not spoken of as empty, though it may be trivial.
10073-8{_}2699{_}"Our ____ was somewhat desultory."
10073-8{_}2700{_}"Thought is less general than ____."
10073-8{_}2701{_}"His ____ was so lively that I had no chance to interrupt" "That is meaningless ____."
10073-8{_}2702{_}<Homesickness, nostalgia>.
10073-8{_}2703{_}All of us have heard physicians call commonplace ailments by extraordinary names.
10073-8{_}2704{_}When homesickness reaches the stage where a physician is or might be called in, it becomes nostalgia.
10073-8{_}2705{_}The latter term suggests morbid or chronic suffering.
10073-8{_}2706{_}A healthy boy away from home for the first time is homesick.
10073-8{_}2707{_}An exile who has wasted himself with pining for his native land is nostalgic.
10073-8{_}2708{_}"His ____ was more than ____; it had so preyed upon his thoughts that it had grown into ____."
10073-8{_}2709{_}Rise, ascend.
10073-8{_}2710{_}_Rise_ is the more general term, but it expresses less than _ascend_ in degree or stateliness.
10073-8{_}2711{_}"He had foretold to them that he would ____ into heaven."
10073-8{_}2712{_}"Do not ____ from your seat."
10073-8{_}2713{_}"The diver slowly ____ to the surface."
10073-8{_}2714{_}"The travelers ____ the mountain."
10073-8{_}2715{_}<Sell, vend>.
10073-8{_}2716{_}_Sell_ is the more dignified word socially, but may express greater moral degradation.
10073-8{_}2717{_}_Vend_ is used of the petty (as that which can be carried about in a wagon), and may suggest the pettily dishonest.
10073-8{_}2718{_}"That man would ____ his country."
10073-8{_}2719{_}"We shall ____ a million dollars' worth of goods."
10073-8{_}2720{_}"The hucksters ____ their wares."
10073-8{_}2721{_}<LIST H>  Study the discriminations between the members of the following pairs.
10073-8{_}2722{_}Determine whether the words are correctly used in the illustrative sentences.
10073-8{_}2723{_}(Some are; some are not.)
10073-8{_}2724{_}<Friendly, amicable>.
10073-8{_}2725{_}_Friendly_ denotes goodwill positive in quality though perhaps limited in degree; we may be friendly to friends, enemies, or strangers.
10073-8{_}2726{_}_Amicable_ is negative, denoting absence of open discord: it is used of those persons between whom some connection already exists.
10073-8{_}2727{_}"The newcomer has an amicable manner."
10073-8{_}2728{_}"Both sides were cautious, but at last they reached a friendly settlement."
10073-8{_}2729{_}"I have only amicable feelings for an enemy who is thus merciful."
10073-8{_}2730{_}"The two met, if not in a friendly, at least in an amicable way."
10073-8{_}2731{_}<Willing, voluntary>.
10073-8{_}2732{_}Both words imply an act of the will; but _willing_ adds positive good-nature, desire, or enthusiasm, whereas _voluntary_ conveys little or nothing of the emotional attitude.
10073-8{_}2733{_}_Voluntary_ is often thought of in contrast with _mechanical_.
10073-8{_}2734{_}"They made willing submission."
10073-8{_}2735{_}"They rendered whole-hearted and voluntary service."
10073-8{_}2736{_}"Though torn by desire to return to his mother, he willingly continued his journey away from her."
10073-8{_}2737{_}"The sneeze was unwilling."
10073-8{_}2738{_}<Greedy, voracious.> _Greedy_ denotes excessiveness (usually habitual) of appetite or, in its figurative uses, of desire; it nearly always carries the idea of selfishness.
10073-8{_}2739{_}_Voracious_ denotes intense hunger or the hasty and prolonged consumption of great quantities of food; it may indicate, not habitual selfishness, but the stress of circumstances.
10073-8{_}2740{_}"Nobody else I know is so greedy as he."
10073-8{_}2741{_}"The young poet was voracious of praise."
10073-8{_}2742{_}"Trench, though a capital fellow, was so hungry that he ate voraciously."
10073-8{_}2743{_}<Offspring, progeny.> _Offspring_ is likely to be used when our thought is chiefly on the children, _progeny_ when our thought is chiefly on the parents.
10073-8{_}2744{_}_Offspring_ may be used of one or many; _progeny_ is used in collective reference to many.
10073-8{_}2745{_}"He was third among the progeny who won distinction."
10073-8{_}2746{_}"They are the progeny of very rich parents."
10073-8{_}2747{_}"Clayton left his offspring well provided for."
10073-8{_}2748{_}<Ghost, spirit.> _Ghost_ is the narrower term.
10073-8{_}2749{_}It never expresses, as _spirit_ does, the idea of soul or of animating mood or purpose.
10073-8{_}2750{_}With reference to incorporeal beings, it denotes (except in the phrase "the Holy Ghost") the reappearance of the dead in disembodied form.
10073-8{_}2751{_}_Spirit_ may denote a variety of incorporeal beings--among them angels, fairies (devoid of moral nature), and personalities returned from the grave and manifested--seldom visibly--through spiritualistic tappings and the like.
10073-8{_}2752{_}"The superstitious natives thought the spirit of their chief walked in the graveyard."
10073-8{_}2753{_}"The ghost of the ancestors survives in the descendants."
10073-8{_}2754{_}"I can call spirits from the vasty deep."
10073-8{_}2755{_}<Foe, enemy.> Nowadays the chief difference between the two terms is that _foe_ is the more used in poetry, _enemy_ in prose.
10073-8{_}2756{_}But _foe_ tends to express the more personal and implacable hostility.
10073-8{_}2757{_}We do not think of foes as bearing any friendship for each other; enemies may, or they may be enemies in public affairs but downright friends in their private relations.
10073-8{_}2758{_}A man is hardly spoken of as being his own foe, but he may be his own enemy.
10073-8{_}2759{_}"For the moment we found ourselves foes."
10073-8{_}2760{_}"Suspicion is an enemy to content."
10073-8{_}2761{_}"I paid a tribute to my friend, who was the dominant personality among the enemy."
10073-8{_}2762{_}<Truth, veracity.> _Truth_ has to do with the accuracy of the statement, of the facts; _veracity_ with the intention of the person to say nothing false.
10073-8{_}2763{_}"I cannot vouch for the veracity of the story, but I can for the truth of the teller."
10073-8{_}2764{_}"Though he is not a man of veracity, I believe he is now speaking the truth."
10073-8{_}2765{_}"Veracity, crushed to earth, will rise again."
10073-8{_}2766{_}<Break, fracture>.
10073-8{_}2767{_}_Break_ is the broader term.
10073-8{_}2768{_}It need not refer clearly to the operation or result of external force, nor need it embody the idea that this force is brought against a hard substance.
10073-8{_}2769{_}In these respects it differs from _fracture_, as also in the fact that it may designate a mere interruption.
10073-8{_}2770{_}Furthermore it has figurative uses, whereas _fracture_ is narrowly literal.
10073-8{_}2771{_}"There was a fracture in the chain of mountains."
10073-8{_}2772{_}"The break in his voice was distinct."
10073-8{_}2773{_}"The fracture of the bones of his wrist incapacitated him."
10073-8{_}2774{_}"The fracture of the rope."
10073-8{_}2775{_}<Hug, embrace>.
10073-8{_}2776{_}To _hug_ is to clasp violently or enthusiastically, and perhaps ludicrously.
10073-8{_}2777{_}To _embrace_ is to clasp in a more dignified, perhaps even in a formal, way; the term also means to include, to comprise.
10073-8{_}2778{_}"This topic embraces the other."
10073-8{_}2779{_}"Did you see that ardent bumpkin embracing his sweetheart?"
10073-8{_}2780{_}"Her sister gave her a graceful but none too cordial hug."
10073-8{_}2781{_}"The wounded bear hugged the hunter ferociously."
10073-8{_}2782{_}<Shorten, abridge>.
10073-8{_}2783{_}The two terms overlap; but there is a fairly strong tendency to use _shorten_ for reduction in length, and _abridge_ for reduction in quantity or mass.
10073-8{_}2784{_}Both words are used figuratively as well as literally.
10073-8{_}2785{_}"The tyrant shortened the privileges of his subjects."
10073-8{_}2786{_}"We shortened the rope."
10073-8{_}2787{_}"The teacher abridged the recitation."
10073-8{_}2788{_}"The report of the committee appears in abridged form in Volume 2 of our records."
10073-8{_}2789{_}<LIST I>  With the help of the dictionary discriminate between the members of the following pairs.

10073-8{_}2795{_}"Your disposition is too inflammable."

10073-8{_}2799{_}"The hot metal was then drawn into an attenuated wire."
10073-8{_}2800{_}"Only a lean line of our soldiers faced the dense masses of the enemy."

10073-8{_}2803{_}"It is home-like, inexpressibly dear."

10073-8{_}2823{_}"We wished for a moment's rest from our exertions."
10073-8{_}2824{_}"Worn out, he was compelled to seek repose."
10073-8{_}2825{_}"Lincoln's face in repose was very melancholy."
10073-8{_}2826{_}<Help, aid>.
10073-8{_}2827{_}"The man was so injured he could do nothing for himself; I had to aid him."
10073-8{_}2828{_}"Help, help!"
10073-8{_}2829{_}"Aid us, O God, in our sore distress."
10073-8{_}2830{_}"The little fellow could not quite get the bundle to his shoulder; a passerby helped him."
10073-8{_}2831{_}<Hide, conceal>.
10073-8{_}2832{_}"By refraining from comment he hid his connection with the affair."
10073-8{_}2833{_}"Wild creatures hide themselves by means of their protective coloring."
10073-8{_}2834{_}"The frost on the panes conceals the landscape from you."
10073-8{_}2835{_}"Do not hide your misdeeds from your mother."
