10073-8{_}2851{_}VII      SYNONYMS IN LARGER GROUPS (1)   In considering pairs we have, without using the word, been studying synonyms.
10073-8{_}2852{_}For most pairs are synonyms (or in some instances antonyms) that hunt in couples.
10073-8{_}2853{_}We must now deal with synonyms, and incidentally antonyms, as they associate themselves in larger groups.
10073-8{_}2854{_}A vocabulary is impoverished.
10073-8{_}2855{_}Why?
10073-8{_}2856{_}Nine times in ten, because of a disregard of synonyms.
10073-8{_}2857{_}Listen to the talk of the average person.
10073-8{_}2858{_}Whatever is pleasing is _fine_ or _nice_ or _all to the good_; whatever is displeasing is _bum_ or _awful_ or _a fright_.
10073-8{_}2859{_}Life is reflected, not as noble and complex, but as mean and meager.
10073-8{_}2860{_}Out of such stereotyped utterance only the general idea emerges.
10073-8{_}2861{_}The precise meaning is lazily or incompetently left to the hearer to imagine.
10073-8{_}2862{_}The precise meaning?
10073-8{_}2863{_}There is none.
10073-8{_}2864{_}A person who does not take the trouble to speak clearly has not taken the trouble to think clearly.
10073-8{_}2865{_}But the master of synonyms expresses, instead of general, hazy, commonplace conceptions, the subtlest shadings of thought and feeling.
10073-8{_}2866{_}He has so trained himself that he selects, it may be unconsciously, from a throng of possible words.
10073-8{_}2867{_}One word may be strong, another weak.
10073-8{_}2868{_}One may be broad, another narrow.
10073-8{_}2869{_}One may present an alternative in meanings, another permit no liberty of choice.
10073-8{_}2870{_}One may be suggestive, another literal or colorless.
10073-8{_}2871{_}One may penetrate to the core of the idea, another strike only in the environs.
10073-8{_}2872{_}With these possibilities the master of synonyms reckons.
10073-8{_}2873{_}He must have the right word.
10073-8{_}2874{_}He chooses it, not at haphazard, but in conformity with a definite purpose.
10073-8{_}2875{_}For synonyms are not words that have the same meaning.
10073-8{_}2876{_}They are words that have similar meanings.
10073-8{_}2877{_}They may be compared to circles that overlap but do not coincide.
10073-8{_}2878{_}Each embraces a common area, but each embraces also an area peculiar to itself.
10073-8{_}2879{_}Though many words cluster about a given idea, rarely if ever are even two of these words entirely equivalent to each other.
10073-8{_}2880{_}In scope, in suggestion, in emotional nuance, in special usage, or what not, is sure to lurk some denial of perfect correspondence.
10073-8{_}2881{_}And of synonyms, so of antonyms.
10073-8{_}2882{_}Antonyms are words opposite in meaning; but the opposition, for the same reasons as the likeness, is seldom or never absolute.
10073-8{_}2883{_}In your study of synonyms you will find most of the dictionaries previously named of great help.
10073-8{_}2884{_}You may also profitably consult the following books of synonyms (heavy, scholastic works not suited for ordinary use are omitted):   <Books of Plain Synonyms and Antonyms>  Edith B. Ordway: _Synonyms and Antonyms_.
10073-8{_}2885{_}A compact, practical volume, with antonyms (in italics for contrast) immediately following synonyms.
10073-8{_}2886{_}Louis A. Flemming: _Putnam's Word Book_.
10073-8{_}2887{_}A book of the ordinarily used synonyms of words, with antonyms after some of them, and with lists of associated words wherever these are likely to be useful.
10073-8{_}2888{_}Samuel Fallows: _100,000 Synonyms and Antonyms_.
10073-8{_}2889{_}A handy little volume, with useful lists of various kinds in appendices.
10073-8{_}2890{_}Richard Soule: _Dictionary of English Synonyms_ [revised and enlarged by George H. Howison].
10073-8{_}2891{_}A much larger and more expensive book than the others, and less practical for ordinary use, but fuller in treatment of material, with words of more than one meaning carefully divided into their various senses.
10073-8{_}2892{_}<Synonyms with Word Discriminations>  George Crabb: _English Synonyms_.
10073-8{_}2893{_}A standard volume for over 100 years.
10073-8{_}2894{_}Has close distinctions, but is somewhat scholarly for ordinary use.
10073-8{_}2895{_}Revised edition of 1917, omitting illustrative quotations from literature, not so good as editions before that date.
10073-8{_}2896{_}James C. Fernald: _English Synonyms, Antonyms, and Prepositions_.
10073-8{_}2897{_}A pleasing book to read, with much information about the use of words and their shades of meaning (with exercises), also with proper prepositions to follow words.
10073-8{_}2898{_}Material taken from the _Standard Dictionary_.
10073-8{_}2899{_}Peter Mark Roget: _Thesaurus of English Words and Phrases_.
10073-8{_}2900{_}Issued in many editions and revisions.
10073-8{_}2901{_}Words grouped under general ideas.
10073-8{_}2902{_}An excellent book for serious and laborious study, but not for quick use.
10073-8{_}2903{_}<How to Acquire Synonyms>  The best principle for the extension of one's mastery of synonyms is the principle already used over and over in this book--that of proceeding from the known to the unknown.
10073-8{_}2904{_}It is the fundamental principle, indeed, of any kind of successful learning.
10073-8{_}2905{_}We should build on what we have, fit each new piece of material into the structure already erected.
10073-8{_}2906{_}But normally it is our ill fortune to learn through chance rather than through system.
10073-8{_}2907{_}We perceive elucidation here, draw an inference there.
10073-8{_}2908{_}These isolated fragments of knowledge may mislead rather than inform us.
10073-8{_}2909{_}The principle of proceeding from the known to the unknown may be applied to synonyms in various ways.
10073-8{_}2910{_}Two of these--the two of most importance--we must consider here.
10073-8{_}2911{_}First, you should reckon with your personal, demonstrated needs.
10073-8{_}2912{_}Just as you have already analyzed your working vocabulary for its general limits and shortcomings, so should you analyze it with particular reference to your poverty in synonyms.
10073-8{_}2913{_}Watch your actual speech; make a list of the words--nouns, verbs, and adjectives particularly--that you employ again and again.
10073-8{_}2914{_}Make each of these words the starting-point for a linguistic exploring expedition.
10073-8{_}2915{_}First, write the word down.
10073-8{_}2916{_}Then under it write all the synonyms that come forthwith to your mind.
10073-8{_}2917{_}These constitute your present available stock; in speaking or writing you could, if you kept yourself mentally alert, summon them on the moment.
10073-8{_}2918{_}But the list, as you know, is not exhaustive.
10073-8{_}2919{_}Draw a line under it and subjoin such synonyms as come to you after reflection.
10073-8{_}2920{_}These constitute a second stock, not instantaneously available, yet to be tagged as among your resources.
10073-8{_}2921{_}Next add a list of the synonyms you find through research, through a ransacking of dictionaries and books of synonyms.
10073-8{_}2922{_}This third stock, but dimly familiar if familiar at all, is in no practical sense yours.
10073-8{_}2923{_}And indeed some of the words are too abstruse, learned, or technical for you to burden your memory with them.
10073-8{_}2924{_}But many--most--are worth acquiring.
10073-8{_}2925{_}By writing down the words of these three classes you have done something to stamp them upon your memory as associates.
10073-8{_}2926{_}You must now make it your business to bring them into use.
10073-8{_}2927{_}Never call upon them for volunteers, but like a wise commander summon the individual that can rightly perform a particular service.
10073-8{_}2928{_}Thus will your speech, perhaps vague and indolent now, become exact, discriminating, competent, vital.
10073-8{_}2929{_}In the second place, you should obtain specific and detailed command of general ideas.
10073-8{_}2930{_}Not of out-of-the-way ideas.
10073-8{_}2931{_}But of the great basic ideas that are the common possession of all mankind.
10073-8{_}2932{_}For through these basic ideas is the most natural and profitable approach to the study of synonyms.
10073-8{_}2933{_}Each of them is represented by a generic word.
10073-8{_}2934{_}So elementary are idea and word alike that a person cannot have the one in mind without having the other ready and a-quiver on his tongue.
10073-8{_}2935{_}Every person is master of both.
10073-8{_}2936{_}But it is unsafe to predicate the person's acquaintance with the shades and phases of the idea, or with the corresponding discriminations in language.
10073-8{_}2937{_}He may not know them at all, he may know them partially, he may know them through and through.
10073-8{_}2938{_}Let us suppose him ignorant of them but determined to learn.
10073-8{_}2939{_}His progress, both in the thought and in the language, will be from the general to the specific.
10073-8{_}2940{_}His acquaintance with the idea in the large he will gradually extend to an acquaintance with it in detail, and his command of the broad term for it he will little by little supplement with definite terms for its phases.
10073-8{_}2941{_}An illustration will make this clear.
10073-8{_}2942{_}We are aware that the world is made up of various classes and conditions of men.
10073-8{_}2943{_}How did we learn this?
10073-8{_}2944{_}Let us go back to the time when our minds were a blank, when we were babes and sucklings, when we had not perceived that men exist, much less that mankind is infinitely complex.
10073-8{_}2945{_}A baby comes slowly to understand that all objects in the universe are divisible into two classes, human and non-human, and that a member of the former may be separated from the others and regarded as an individual.
10073-8{_}2946{_}It has reached the initial stage of its knowledge on the subject; it has the basic idea, that of the individual human being.
10073-8{_}2947{_}As soon as it can speak, it acquires a designating term--not of course the sophisticated _human being_, but the simpler _man_.
10073-8{_}2948{_}It uses this word in the generic sense, to indicate _any_ member of the human race; for as yet it knows nothing and cares nothing about differences in species.
10073-8{_}2949{_}With increasing enlightenment, however, it discerns five species, and distinguishes among them by swelling this branch of its vocabulary to five words: man (in the sense of adult male), woman, boy, girl, baby.
10073-8{_}2950{_}(To be sure, it may chance to have acquired a specific term, as _boy_ or _baby_, before the generic term _man_; but if so, it has attached this term to some particular individual, as the grocer's boy or itself, rather than to the individuals of a species.
10073-8{_}2951{_}Its understanding of the species as a species comes after its understanding of the genus.)
10073-8{_}2952{_}As time passes, it divides mankind into yet further species by sundry other methods: according to occupation, for example, as doctors, chauffeurs, gardeners; to race or color, as white men; negroes, Malays, Chinese; to disposition, as heroes, gift-givers, teasers, talkers; and so on.
10073-8{_}2953{_}It perceives moreover that species are made up of sub-species.
10073-8{_}2954{_}Thus instead of lumping all boys together it begins to distinguish them as big boys, little boys, middle-sized boys, boys in long trousers, boys in short trousers, barefoot boys, schoolboys, poor boys, rich boys, sick boys, well boys, friends, enemies, bullies, and what not.
10073-8{_}2955{_}It even divides the sub-species.
10073-8{_}2956{_}Thus it classifies schoolboys as bright boys, dullards, workers, shirkers, teachers' favorites, scapegoats, athletes, note-throwers, truant-players, and the like.
10073-8{_}2957{_}And of these classes it may make yet further sub-divisions, or at least it may separate them into the individuals that compose them.
10073-8{_}2958{_}In fine, with its growing powers and experience, it abandons its old conception that all persons are practically alike, and follows human nature through the countless ramifications of man's status, temperament, activities, or fate.
10073-8{_}2959{_}And it augments its vocabulary to keep pace, roughly at least, with its expanding ideas.
10073-8{_}2960{_}In thought and terminology alike its growth is from genus to species.
10073-8{_}2961{_}So it is with all our ideas and with all our words to cap them.
10073-8{_}2962{_}We radiate from an ascertained center into new areas of knowledge; we proceed from the broad, fundamental, generic to the precise, discriminatory, specific.
10073-8{_}2963{_}Upon this natural law are based the exercises in this chapter and the two to follow.
10073-8{_}2964{_}The starting-point is always a word representative of an elementary idea--a word and an idea which everybody knows; the advance is into the unknown or the unused, at any rate into the particular.
10073-8{_}2965{_}Now fundamental ideas are not very numerous, and these exercises include the commoner ones.
10073-8{_}2966{_}Such a method of studying synonyms must therefore yield large and tangible results.
10073-8{_}2967{_}One matter, however, should be explained.
10073-8{_}2968{_}Most books of synonyms start with a word and list all the terms in any way related to it.
10073-8{_}2969{_}The idea of the compilers is that the more they give the student the more they help him.
10073-8{_}2970{_}But oftentimes by giving more than is strictly pertinent they actually hinder and confuse him.
10073-8{_}2971{_}They may do this in various ways, of which two must be mentioned.
10073-8{_}2972{_}First, they follow an idea too far afield.
10073-8{_}2973{_}Thus in listing the synonyms of _love_ they include such terms as _kindness_ and _lenity_, words only through stretched usage connected with _love_.
10073-8{_}2974{_}Secondly, they trace, not one meaning of a word, but two or more unrelated meanings when the word chances to possess them.
10073-8{_}2975{_}Thus in listing the synonyms of _cry_ they include both the idea of weeping and the idea of calling or screaming.
10073-8{_}2976{_}What are the results of these methods?
10073-8{_}2977{_}The student finds a clutter where he expects rationalized order; he finds he must exclude many words which lie in the borders and fringes of the meaning.
10073-8{_}2978{_}Moreover he finds mere chance associations mingled with marked kinships.
10073-8{_}2979{_}In both cases he finds dulled distinctions.
10073-8{_}2980{_}This book offers synonyms that are apropos and definite rather than comprehensive.
10073-8{_}2981{_}Starting with a basic idea, it finds the generic term; it then disregards dim and distant relationships, confines itself rigorously to one of perhaps two or three legitimate senses, and refuses to consider the peculiar twists and devious ways of subsidiary words when they wander from the idea it is tracing.
10073-8{_}2982{_}It thus deliberately blinds itself to much that is interesting.
10073-8{_}2983{_}But this partial blindness enables it to concentrate attention upon the matter actually under study, to give sharper distinctions and surer guidance.
10073-8{_}2984{_}EXERCISE A  After three introductory groups (dealing with thoroughly concrete ideas and words) the synonyms in this exercise are arranged alphabetically according to the first word in each group.
10073-8{_}2985{_}This first word is generic.
10073-8{_}2986{_}It is immediately followed by a list of its synonyms.
10073-8{_}2987{_}These are then informally discriminated or else (in a few instances) questions are asked about them.
10073-8{_}2988{_}Perhaps a few less closely related synonyms are then listed for you to discriminate in a similar way.
10073-8{_}2989{_}Finally, illustrative sentences are given.
10073-8{_}2990{_}Each blank in these you are to fill with the word that conveys the meaning exactly.
10073-8{_}2991{_}(To prevent monotony and inattention, the number of illustrative sentences varies.
10073-8{_}2992{_}You may have to use a particular word more than once, and another word not at all.)
10073-8{_}2993{_}<Walk, plod, trudge, tread, stride, stalk, strut, tramp, march, pace, toddle, waddle, shuffle, mince, stroll, saunter, ramble, meander, promenade, prowl, hobble, limp, perambulate.>  Any one may be said to _walk_ who moves along on foot with moderate speed.
10073-8{_}2994{_}He _plods_ if he walks slowly and heavily, and perhaps monotonously or spiritlessly as well.
10073-8{_}2995{_}He _trudges_ if he walks toilsomely and wearily, as though his feet were heavy.
10073-8{_}2996{_}He _treads_ if his walk is suggestive of a certain lightness and caution--if, for instance, he seems half-uncertain whether to proceed and sets one foot down carefully before the other.
10073-8{_}2997{_}He _strides_ if he takes long steps, especially in a firm, pompous, or lofty manner.
10073-8{_}2998{_}He _stalks_ if there is a certain stiffness or haughtiness in his walking.
10073-8{_}2999{_}He _struts_ if he walks with a proud or affectedly dignified gait, especially if he also raises his feet high.
10073-8{_}3000{_}He _tramps_ if he goes for a long walk, as for pleasure or enjoyment out-of-doors.
10073-8{_}3001{_}He _marches_ if he walks in a measured, ordered way, especially in company with others.
10073-8{_}3002{_}He _paces_ if he engages in a measured, continuous walk, as from nervousness, impatience, or anger.
10073-8{_}3003{_}He _toddles_ if his steps are short, uneven, and unsteady, like those of a child.
10073-8{_}3004{_}He _waddles_ if his movement is ungainly, with a duck-like swaying from side to side.
10073-8{_}3005{_}He _shuffles_ if he drags his feet with a scraping noise.
10073-8{_}3006{_}He _minces_ if he takes short steps in a prim, precise, or affectedly nice manner.
10073-8{_}3007{_}He _strolls_ or _saunters_ if he goes along in an easy, aimless, or idle fashion.
10073-8{_}3008{_}He _rambles_ if he wanders about, with no definite aim or toward no definite goal.
10073-8{_}3009{_}He _meanders_ if he proceeds slowly and perhaps listlessly in an ever-changing course, as if he were following the windings of the crooked Phrygian river, Meander.
10073-8{_}3010{_}He _promenades_ if he walks in a public place, as for pleasure or display.
10073-8{_}3011{_}He _prowls_ if he moves about softly and stealthily, as in search of prey or booty.
10073-8{_}3012{_}He _hobbles_ if he jerks along unevenly, as from a stiff or crippled condition of body.
10073-8{_}3013{_}He _limps_ if he walks lamely.
10073-8{_}3014{_}He _perambulates_ when he walks through, perhaps for observation or inspection.
10073-8{_}3015{_}_(Perambulates is_ of course a learned word.)
10073-8{_}3016{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: <sneak, shamble, amble, wander, stamp, slouch, gad, gallivant, glide, hike>.
10073-8{_}3017{_}_Sentences_: They ____ down the lane in the moonlight.
10073-8{_}3018{_}Rip Van Winkle loved to ____ about the mountains.
10073-8{_}3019{_}"The plowman homeward ____ his weary way."
10073-8{_}3020{_}The old man ____ down the street with his cane.
10073-8{_}3021{_}The excavators ____ about the ruins in search of relics.
10073-8{_}3022{_}He ____ about the room, almost bursting with importance.
10073-8{_}3023{_}The nervous man ____ up and down the station platform.
10073-8{_}3024{_}They ____ along the beach at the sea resort.
10073-8{_}3025{_}The baby learned to ____ when it was eleven months old.
10073-8{_}3026{_}The two of them ____ about the field all day hunting rabbits.
10073-8{_}3027{_}A ghost, so they tell me, ____ about the haunted house at midnight.
10073-8{_}3028{_}He carefully ____ the plank that spans the abyss.
10073-8{_}3029{_}The baby ____ toward us with outstretched arms.
10073-8{_}3030{_}The Chinaman ____ out of the back room of the laundry in his carpet slippers.
10073-8{_}3031{_}They caught glimpses of gaunt wolves ____ about their campfire.
10073-8{_}3032{_}He was terrified when the giant ____ into the room.
10073-8{_}3033{_}The fat lady ____ down the aisle of the street car.
10073-8{_}3034{_}The sick man will ____ a few steps each day until he is stronger.
10073-8{_}3035{_}A turkey cock ____ about the barnyard.
10073-8{_}3036{_}A boy with a rag tied around his toe ____ painfully down the street.
10073-8{_}3037{_}They reported to the police that a man had been ____ about the place.
10073-8{_}3038{_}She held her skirts daintily and ____ along as if she were walking on eggs.
10073-8{_}3039{_}The lovers ____ along the banks of the stream.
10073-8{_}3040{_}He ____ through the hall like a conqueror.
10073-8{_}3041{_}The children wore themselves out by ____ through the snow to school.
10073-8{_}3042{_}We ____ through the meadows, often stooping to pick flowers as we went.
10073-8{_}3043{_}The soldiers ____ into camp at nightfall.
10073-8{_}3044{_}<Laugh, giggle, snicker, titter, chuckle, guffaw, cachinnate.>  What differences in human nature, conditions, and disposition are revealed by laughter!
10073-8{_}3045{_}If a person gives audible expression to mirth, gayety, or good-humor, the simplest word to apply to what he does is _laugh_.
10073-8{_}3046{_}But suppose a girl, with slight or insufficient provocation, engages in silly or foolish though perhaps involuntary laughter.
10073-8{_}3047{_}We should say she _giggles_.
10073-8{_}3048{_}Suppose a youngster is amused at an inappropriate moment and but partly suppresses his laughter; or suppose he wilfully permits the breaking forth of just enough laughter to indicate disrespect.
10073-8{_}3049{_}He _snickers_.
10073-8{_}3050{_}Suppose a person gives a little, light laugh; or more especially, suppose a crowd gives such an one as the result of slight, simultaneous amusement.
10073-8{_}3051{_}Our word now is _titters_.
10073-8{_}3052{_}Suppose we laugh low or gently or to ourselves.
10073-8{_}3053{_}We _chuckle_.
10073-8{_}3054{_}Suppose some one laughs loudly, boisterously, even coarsely, in a manner befitting a lumber camp rather than a drawing room.
10073-8{_}3055{_}That person _guffaws_.
10073-8{_}3056{_}Suppose a man engages in explosive and immoderate laughter.
10073-8{_}3057{_}He _cachinnates_.
10073-8{_}3058{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: <chortle, roar>.
10073-8{_}3059{_}_Second assignment_: Name all the words you can that designate inaudible laughter (for example, <smile, smirk, grin>).
10073-8{_}3060{_}_Sentences_: The rough fellow ____ in the lecturer's face.
10073-8{_}3061{_}"If you prick us, do we not bleed?
10073-8{_}3062{_}if you tickle us, do we not ____?"
10073-8{_}3063{_}He kept ____ at the thought of the surprise he would give them.
10073-8{_}3064{_}"The swain mistrustless of his smutted face, While secret laughter ____ round the place."
10073-8{_}3065{_}The ill-bred fellow was ____ with strident, violent, irritating sounds.
10073-8{_}3066{_}"The little dog ____ to see such sport."
10073-8{_}3067{_}The audience ____ when the speaker's glasses began to slip from his nose.
10073-8{_}3068{_}The girl kept ____ in a way that embarrassed us both.
10073-8{_}3069{_}The small boy ____ when the preacher's notes fluttered out of the Bible to the floor.
10073-8{_}3070{_}The rude fellows ____ at this evidence of my discomfiture.
10073-8{_}3071{_}He ____ very kindly and told me not to feel any regrets.
10073-8{_}3072{_}The little maids tried to be polite, but ____ irrepressibly.
10073-8{_}3073{_}<Look, glance, gaze, stare, peer, scan, scrutinize, gloat, glare, glower, lower, peek, peep, gape, con, pore, ogle.>  A person simply directs his eyes to see.
10073-8{_}3074{_}He _looks_.
10073-8{_}3075{_}But eyes may speak, we are told, and since this person undergoes many changes of mood and purpose, we shall let his eyes tell us all they will about his different manners of looking.
10073-8{_}3076{_}At first he but looks momentarily (as from lack of time) or casually (as from lack of interest).
10073-8{_}3077{_}He _glances_.
10073-8{_}3078{_}Soon he makes a business of looking, and fastens his eyes for a long time on something he admires or wonders at.
10073-8{_}3079{_}He _gazes_.
10073-8{_}3080{_}Presently he looks with a blank, perhaps a rude, expression and with eyes opened widely; he may be for the moment overcome with incomprehension, surprise, or fright, or perhaps he wishes to be insolent.
10073-8{_}3081{_}He _stares_.
10073-8{_}3082{_}Now he is looking narrowly or closely at something that he sees with difficulty.
10073-8{_}3083{_}He _peers_.
10073-8{_}3084{_}The next moment he looks over something with care or with an encompassing sweep of vision.
10073-8{_}3085{_}He _scans_ it.
10073-8{_}3086{_}His interest thoroughly enlisted, he looks at it carefully point by point to see that it is right in each detail.
10073-8{_}3087{_}He _scrutinizes_ it.
10073-8{_}3088{_}He then alters his mood, and looks with scornful or malignant satisfaction upon something he has conquered or has power over.
10073-8{_}3089{_}He _gloats_.
10073-8{_}3090{_}Anger, perhaps fierceness, takes possession of him, and he looks with piercing eyes.
10073-8{_}3091{_}He _glares_.
10073-8{_}3092{_}Threat mingles with anger, and in all likelihood he looks scowlingly or frowningly.
10073-8{_}3093{_}He _glowers_.
10073-8{_}3094{_}An added expression of sullenness or gloom comes into his look.
10073-8{_}3095{_}He _lowers_.
10073-8{_}3096{_}He throws off his dark spirit and looks slyly and playfully, let us say through a small opening.
10073-8{_}3097{_}He _peeks_.
10073-8{_}3098{_}Playfulness gives place to curiosity; he looks quickly and furtively, perhaps through some tiny aperture, and probably at something he has no business to see.
10073-8{_}3099{_}He _peeps_.
10073-8{_}3100{_}The while he looks his mouth falls open, as from stupidity or wonder.
10073-8{_}3101{_}He _gapes_.
10073-8{_}3102{_}He looks at something a long time to study it.
10073-8{_}3103{_}He _cons_ or _pores_.
10073-8{_}3104{_}His study is not of the thing itself; it is meditation or reverie.
10073-8{_}3105{_}He _pores_.
10073-8{_}3106{_}A member of the opposite sex is present; he looks at her with the effort of a flirt to attract attention to himself, or less scrupulous, he directs toward her amorous or inviting glances.
10073-8{_}3107{_}He _ogles_.
10073-8{_}3108{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: <leer, view, survey, inspect, regard, watch, contemplate>.
10073-8{_}3109{_}_Sentences:_ The inspecting officer ____ the men's equipment.
10073-8{_}3110{_}The student ____ his lessons carefully.
10073-8{_}3111{_}At this unexpected proposal Dobbett merely ____.
10073-8{_}3112{_}Jimmie ____ at the fellow who had kicked the pup.
10073-8{_}3113{_}The inquisitive maid ____ into all the the closets.
10073-8{_}3114{_}He ____ over his fallen adversary.
10073-8{_}3115{_}The bookkeeper ____ over his ledger.
10073-8{_}3116{_}In the darkened hallway he ____ at the notices on the bulletin board.
10073-8{_}3117{_}"The poet's eye, in a fine frenzy rolling, Doth ____ from heaven to earth, from earth to heaven."
10073-8{_}3118{_}From the way her father ____ the foolish, young man should have known it was time to go.
10073-8{_}3119{_}He ____ long and lovingly upon the scenes he was leaving.
10073-8{_}3120{_}The newcomer ____ insolently at his host and ____ the young ladies.
10073-8{_}3121{_}<Abandon, desert, forsake.>  _Abandon_ denotes absolute giving up, as from force of circumstances or shirking of responsibility.
10073-8{_}3122{_}_Desert_ refers to leaving or quitting in violation of obligation, duty, or oath.
10073-8{_}3123{_}_Forsake_, which may involve no culpability, usually implies a breaking off of intimate association or attachment.
10073-8{_}3124{_}_Sentences_: The sailor ____ his ship.
10073-8{_}3125{_}Necessity compelled him to ____ his friends in a time of sore trouble.
10073-8{_}3126{_}They hated to ____ their old haunts.
10073-8{_}3127{_}A brave man never ____ hope.
10073-8{_}3128{_}An unscrupulous man will ____ his principles when it is to his advantage.
10073-8{_}3129{_}"When my father and my mother ____ me, then the Lord will take me up."
10073-8{_}3130{_}We ____ our attempt to save the ship.
10073-8{_}3131{_}<Abase, debase, degrade, humble, humiliate, disgrace.>  To _abase_ is to bring down so that the victim feels himself lowered in estate or external condition.
10073-8{_}3132{_}To _debase_ is to produce a marked decline in actual worth or in moral quality.
10073-8{_}3133{_}To _degrade_ is to lower in rank or status.
10073-8{_}3134{_}To _humble_ is to lower in dignity or self-esteem, or as used reflexively, to restrain one's own pride; the word often implies that the person has been over-proud or arrogant.
10073-8{_}3135{_}To _humiliate_ is to deprive of self-esteem or to bring into ignominy.
10073-8{_}3136{_}To _disgrace_ is to bring actual shame upon.
10073-8{_}3137{_}_Sentences_: They ____ the guilty officer from captain to lieutenant.
10073-8{_}3138{_}A man should ____ himself before God.
10073-8{_}3139{_}He had so ____ himself that I no longer expected good of him.
10073-8{_}3140{_}His detection at cheating had ____ him before the students.
10073-8{_}3141{_}By successive overlords they had been ____ into a condition of serfdom.
10073-8{_}3142{_}The aristocratic old lady was ____ by her loss of social position.
10073-8{_}3143{_}The conversion of so much bullion into money had ____ the coinage.
10073-8{_}3144{_}<Answer, reply, response, rejoinder, retort, repartee.>  An interesting thing about the _answer_ group is that the generic term has a somewhat strong rival in _reply_, itself fairly inclusive.
10073-8{_}3145{_}We must therefore discriminate rather fully between _answer_ and _reply_.
10073-8{_}3146{_}The former is a return in words to a question, a communication, or an argument.
10073-8{_}3147{_}The latter suggests a more or less formal answer, as one carefully prepared or intelligently thought out.
10073-8{_}3148{_}We might give an _answer_ offhand, but are less likely to give a _reply_ so.
10073-8{_}3149{_}We may give any kind of _answer_ to a question, but if we give a _reply_, the implication is that we have answered it definitely, perhaps satisfactorily.
10073-8{_}3150{_}On the other hand, in controversial matters we may, though we by no means always do, imply a more conclusive meeting of objections through _answer_ than through _reply_.
10073-8{_}3151{_}A _response_ is an expected answer, one in harmony with the question or assertion, or in some way carrying the thought farther.
10073-8{_}3152{_}A _rejoinder_ is a quick reply to something controversial or calling forth opposition.
10073-8{_}3153{_}A _retort_ is a short, sharp reply, such as turns back censure or derision, or as springs from anger.
10073-8{_}3154{_}A _repartee_ is an immediate and witty reply, perhaps to a remark of similar character which it is intended to surpass in cleverness.

10073-8{_}3156{_}The plays of Oscar Wilde abound in brilliant ____.

10073-8{_}3166{_}<Ask, inquire, question, interrogate, interpellate, query, quiz, catechize, request, beg, solicit, entreat, beseech, crave, implore, supplicate, importune, petition.>  From the general tree of asking grow many branches, different in size, in the direction they take, in the shades of meaning they cast.
10073-8{_}3167{_}What can we learn from a rapid scrutiny of each?
10073-8{_}3168{_}That to _inquire_ is to ask for specific information.
10073-8{_}3169{_}That to _question_ is to keep asking in order to obtain detailed or reluctantly given information.
10073-8{_}3170{_}That to _interrogate_ is to question formally, systematically, or thoroughly.
10073-8{_}3171{_}That to _interpellate_ is to question as of unchallenged right, as in a deliberative body.
10073-8{_}3172{_}That to _query_ is to bring a thing into question because of doubt as to its correctness or truth.
10073-8{_}3173{_}That to _quiz_is to question closely and persistently, as from meddlesomeness, opposition, or curiosity.
10073-8{_}3174{_}That to _catechize is_ to question in a minute, perhaps impertinent, manner in order to ascertain one's secrets or the amount of his knowledge or information.
10073-8{_}3175{_}That to _request_ is to ask formally and politely.
10073-8{_}3176{_}That to _beg_ is to ask for deferentially or humbly, especially on the ground of pity.
10073-8{_}3177{_}That to _solicit_ is to ask with urgency.
10073-8{_}3178{_}That to _entreat_ is to ask with strong desire and moving appeal.
10073-8{_}3179{_}That to _beseech_ is to ask earnestly as a boon or favor.
10073-8{_}3180{_}That to _crave_ is to ask humbly and abjectly, as though unworthy of receiving.
10073-8{_}3181{_}That to _implore_ is to ask with fervor and intense earnestness.
10073-8{_}3182{_}That to _supplicate_ is to ask with urgent or even desperate appeal.
10073-8{_}3183{_}(Both _implore_ and _supplicate_ imply humility, as of a prayer to a superior being.)
10073-8{_}3184{_}That to _importune_ is to ask for persistently, even wearyingly.
10073-8{_}3185{_}That to _petition_ is to ask a superior, usually in writing, for some favor, grant, or right.
10073-8{_}3186{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: <plead, pray>.

10073-8{_}3210{_}<Burn, scorch, singe, sear, parch, char, incinerate, cremate, cauterize.>  If you consume or injure something by bringing it in contact with fire or heat, you _burn_ it.
10073-8{_}3211{_}If you do not consume it but burn it superficially so as to change the texture or color of its surface, you _scorch_ it.
10073-8{_}3212{_}If you burn off ends or projections of it, you _singe_ it.
10073-8{_}3213{_}If you burn its surface to dryness or hardness, you _sear_ it.
10073-8{_}3214{_}If you dry or shrivel it with heat, you _parch_ it.
10073-8{_}3215{_}If through heat you reduce it to a state of charcoal, or cinders, you _char_ it.
10073-8{_}3216{_}If you burn it to ashes, you _incinerate_ it.
10073-8{_}3217{_}(This word is learned and but little used in ordinary discourse.)
10073-8{_}3218{_}If you burn a dead body to ashes, you _cremate_ it.
10073-8{_}3219{_}If you burn or sear anything with a hot iron or a corrosive substance, you _cauterize_ it.

10073-8{_}3228{_}<Busy, industrious, diligent, assiduous, sedulous.>  From much of the talk that we hear nowadays it might be supposed that the earnest devotion of one's self to a task is a thing that has disappeared from the earth.

10073-8{_}3232{_}The man who makes continued application to work a principle or habit of life, is _industrious_.
10073-8{_}3233{_}The man who applies himself aggressively to the accomplishment of some specific undertaking or pursuit, is _diligent_.
10073-8{_}3234{_}The man who quietly and determinedly sticks to a task until it is accomplished, no matter what its difficulties or length, is _assiduous_.
10073-8{_}3235{_}The man who makes steady and painstaking application to whatever he is about, is _sedulous_.

10073-8{_}3243{_}<Concise, terse, succinct, compendious, compact, sententious, pithy, laconic, curt.>  Words descriptive of brief utterance are, in nearly every instance, in their origin figurative.
10073-8{_}3244{_}The brevity is brought out by comparison with something that is noticeably short or small.
10073-8{_}3245{_}Let us examine the words of our list for their figurative qualities.
10073-8{_}3246{_}A _concise_ statement is one that is _cut down_ until a great deal is said in a few words.
10073-8{_}3247{_}A _terse_ statement is _rubbed off_, rid of unessentials.
10073-8{_}3248{_}A _succinct_ statement has its important thoughts _bound_ into small compass, as by a girdle.
10073-8{_}3249{_}A _compendious_ statement _weighs together_ the various thoughts and aspects of a subject; it shows by means of a few effective words just what these amount to, gives a summary of them.
10073-8{_}3250{_}A _compact_ statement has its units of thought _fastened together_ into firmness of structure; its brevity is well-knit.
10073-8{_}3251{_}A _sententious_ statement gives _feelings_ or opinions_ in a strikingly pointed or axiomatic way, so that they can be easily grasped and remembered; if _sententious_ is unfavorably used, the statement may be filled with paraded platitudes.
10073-8{_}3252{_}A _pithy_ statement gives the very _pith_, the heart of a matter; it is sometimes slightly quaint, always effective and arresting.
10073-8{_}3253{_}A _laconic_ statement is made in the manner of _the Spartans_, who hated talk and used as few words as possible.
10073-8{_}3254{_}A _curt_ statement is _made short_; its abruptness is oftentimes more or less rude.

10073-8{_}3265{_}It is not too good for a clown or too poor for an emperor.
10073-8{_}3266{_}_Decease_ is a more formal word.
10073-8{_}3267{_}Its employment is often legal--the death proves to be of sufficient importance for the law (and the lawyers) to take notice.
10073-8{_}3268{_}_Demise_, however, is outwardly the most resplendent term of all.

10073-8{_}3270{_}It is used of an illustrious person, as a king, who transmits his title to an heir.

10073-8{_}3272{_}If the term is applied to their shuffling off of this mortal coil, the use is euphemistic and likely to be stilted.

10073-8{_}3332{_}<Foretell, predict, prophesy, forecast, presage, forebode, portend, augur, prognosticate.>  _Foretell_ is the general word for stating or perceiving beforehand that which will happen.

10073-8{_}3338{_}_Forebode_ expresses an uncertain foreknowledge of vague impending evil.

10073-8{_}3340{_}_Augur_ means foretelling from omens.

10073-8{_}3348{_}<Get, acquire, obtain, procure, attain, gain, win, earn.>  _Get_, the general term, may be used of whatever one comes by whatsoever means to possess, experience, or realize.
10073-8{_}3349{_}To _acquire_ is to get into more or less permanent possession, either by some gradual process or by one's determined efforts.
10073-8{_}3350{_}To _obtain_ is to get something desired by means of deliberate effort or request.
10073-8{_}3351{_}To _procure_ is to get by definitely planned effort something which, in most instances, is of a temporary nature or the possession of which is temporary.
10073-8{_}3352{_}To _attain_ is to get through striving that which one has set as a goal or end of his desire or ambition.
10073-8{_}3353{_}To _gain_ is to get that which is advantageous.
10073-8{_}3354{_}To _win_ is to get as the result of successful competition or the overcoming of opposition.
10073-8{_}3355{_}To _earn_ is to get as a deserved reward for one's efforts or exertions.

10073-8{_}3401{_}<Hate, detest, abhor, loathe, abominate, despise>.
10073-8{_}3402{_}To _hate_ involves deep or passionate dislike, sometimes bred of ill-will.
10073-8{_}3403{_}To _detest_ involves an intense, vehement, or deep-seated antipathy.
10073-8{_}3404{_}To _abhor_ involves utter repugnance or aversion, with an impulse to recoil.
10073-8{_}3405{_}To _loathe_ involves disgust because of physical or moral offensiveness.
10073-8{_}3406{_}To _abominate_ involves strong moral aversion, as of that which is odious or wicked.
10073-8{_}3407{_}To _despise_ is to dislike and look down upon as inferior.

10073-8{_}3417{_}<Healthful, wholesome, salutary, salubrious, sanitary, hygienic>.
10073-8{_}3418{_}(With this group contrast the _Disease_ group below.)
10073-8{_}3419{_}The words of this group are assuredly blessed.
10073-8{_}3420{_}Every one of them has to do with the giving, promotion, or preservation of health.
10073-8{_}3421{_}But health is of various kinds, and therefore the words apply differently.
10073-8{_}3422{_}_Healthful_ is the most inclusive of them; it means that the thing it refers to is full of health for us.
10073-8{_}3423{_}_Wholesome_ also is a very broad term; what is wholesome is good for us physically, mentally, or morally.
10073-8{_}3424{_}_Salutary_ is confined to that which affects for good our moral (including civic and social) welfare, especially if it counteracts evil influences or propensities.
10073-8{_}3425{_}_Salubrious_ is confined to the physical; it is used almost solely of healthful air or climate.
10073-8{_}3426{_}_Sanitary_ and _hygienic_ apply to physical well-being as promoted by the eradication of the causes for sickness, disease, or the like; _sanitary_, however, is used of measures and conditions affecting people in general, whereas _hygienic_ connects itself with personal habits.
10073-8{_}3427{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: The word _healthy_ is often confused with _healthful_.
10073-8{_}3428{_}You have already discriminated between these two terms, but you should renew your knowledge of the distinction between them.

10073-8{_}3443{_}<Masculine, male, manly, manlike, manful, mannish, virile>.

10073-8{_}3447{_}_Male_ designates sex and is used of animals as well as human beings.
10073-8{_}3448{_}_Manly_ (used of boys as well as men) implies the possession of qualities worthy of a man, as strength, courage, sincerity, honesty, independence, or even tenderness.
10073-8{_}3449{_}_Manlike_ refers to qualities, attributes, or foibles characteristically masculine.
10073-8{_}3450{_}_Manful_ suggests the valor, prowess, or resolution properly belonging to men.
10073-8{_}3451{_}_Mannish_ (a derogatory word) indicates superficial or affected qualities of manhood, especially when inappropriately possessed by a woman.
10073-8{_}3452{_}_Virile_ applies to the sturdy and intrepid qualities of mature manhood.

10073-8{_}3465{_}<Name, appellation, designation, denomination, title, alias>.
10073-8{_}3466{_}A _name_ is the word or words by which a person or thing is called or known.
10073-8{_}3467{_}If the name be descriptive or characterizing, even though in a fanciful way, it is an _appellation_.
10073-8{_}3468{_}If it particularizes an individual through reference to distinctive quality or nature, perhaps without employing any word the individual is usually known by, it is a _designation_.
10073-8{_}3469{_}If it specifies a class, especially a religious sect or a kind of coin, it is a _denomination_.
10073-8{_}3470{_}If it is an official or honorary description of rank, office, place within a profession, or the like, it is a _title_.
10073-8{_}3471{_}If it is assumed, as to conceal identity, it is an _alias_.

10073-8{_}3486{_}<Old, ancient, olden, antique, antiquated, archaic, obsolete, venerable, immemorial, elderly, aged, hoary, decrepit, senile, superannuated>.
10073-8{_}3487{_}We reserve the right to judge for ourselves when told that something-- especially a joke--is "the very latest."
10073-8{_}3488{_}So may we likewise discriminate among degrees of age.
10073-8{_}3489{_}_Old_ is applied to a person or thing that has existed for a long time or that existed in the distant past.
10073-8{_}3490{_}The word may suggest a familiarity or sentiment not found in _ancient_, which is used of that which lived or happened in the remote past, or has come down from it.
10073-8{_}3491{_}_Olden_ applies almost wholly to time long past.
10073-8{_}3492{_}_Antique_ is the term for that which has come down from ancient times or is made in imitation of the style of ancient times, whereas _antiquated_ is the term for that which has gone out of style or fashion.
10073-8{_}3493{_}_Archaic_ and _obsolete_ refer to words, customs, or the like, the former to such as savor of an earlier period though they are not yet completely out of use, the latter to such as have passed out of use altogether.
10073-8{_}3494{_}_Immemorial_ implies that a thing is so old that it is beyond the time of memory or record.
10073-8{_}3495{_}_Elderly_ is applied to persons who are between middle age and old age.
10073-8{_}3496{_}_Aged_ is used of one who has lived for an unusually long time.
10073-8{_}3497{_}_Hoary_ refers to age as revealed by white hair.
10073-8{_}3498{_}_Venerable_ suggests the reverence to be paid to the dignity, goodness, or wisdom of old age.
10073-8{_}3499{_}_Decrepit_ conveys a sense of the physical infirmities and weakness which attend old age; _senile_ of the lessening powers of both body and mind that result from old age.
10073-8{_}3500{_}_Superannuated_ is applied to a person who on account of old age has been declared incapable of continuing his activities.

10073-8{_}3524{_}<Pay, compensate, recompense, remunerate, requite, reimburse, indemnify>.
10073-8{_}3525{_}Most men are willing to receive what is due them.
10073-8{_}3526{_}They might even be persuaded to receive a bit more.

10073-8{_}3528{_}Sometimes they are.
10073-8{_}3529{_}Offering to _pay_ some people instead of to _compensate_ them is like offering a tip to the wrong person.
10073-8{_}3530{_}Why?
10073-8{_}3531{_}Because there is a social implication in _compensate_ which is not contained in _pay_.
10073-8{_}3532{_}To _pay_ is simply to give what is due, as in wages (or even salary), price, or the like.
10073-8{_}3533{_}To _compensate_ is to make suitable return for service rendered.
10073-8{_}3534{_}Does _compensate_ not sound the more soothing?
10073-8{_}3535{_}But save in exceptional circumstances the downrightness of _pay_ has no hint of vulgarity.
10073-8{_}3536{_}To _recompense_ is to make a return, especially if it is not monetary, for work, pains, trouble, losses, or suffering; or some quality or blessing (as affection or happiness) may be said to recompense one.
10073-8{_}3537{_}To _remunerate_ is to disburse a large amount to a person, or to give it to him as a reward, or otherwise to make him a return in a matter of importance.
10073-8{_}3538{_}To _requite_ is to put a just value upon one's work, deeds, or merit and to make payment strictly in accordance with his deserts.
10073-8{_}3539{_}To _reimburse_ is to make good what some one has spent for you.
10073-8{_}3540{_}To _indemnify_ is to secure some one against loss or to make restitution for damages he has sustained.
10073-8{_}3541{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: <disburse, reward>.

10073-8{_}3550{_}<Proud, arrogant, presumptuous, haughty, supercilious, insolent, insulting>.
10073-8{_}3551{_}Most persons feel in their hearts that their claims and merits are superior to those of other people.
10073-8{_}3552{_}But they do not like for you, in describing them, to imply that their self-appraisal is too high.
10073-8{_}3553{_}"Comparisons are odious," and therefore in comparing their fancied with their real selves you must choose your terms carefully.
10073-8{_}3554{_}Of the words that suggest an exaggerated estimate of one's merits or privileges the broadest, as well as the least offensive, is _proud_.
10073-8{_}3555{_}In fact this word need not carry the idea of exaggeration.

10073-8{_}3557{_}On the other hand, he may overvalue his attainments, possessions, connections, etc.
10073-8{_}3558{_}To say that the man is _arrogant_ means that he combines with pride a contempt for others, that he claims for himself greater attention, consideration, or respect than he is entitled to.
10073-8{_}3559{_}To say that he is _presumptuous_ makes him an inferior (or at least not a superior) who claims privileges or takes liberties improperly.
10073-8{_}3560{_}To say that he is _haughty_ means that he assumes a disdainful superiority to others, especially through fancied or actual advantage over them in birth or social position.
10073-8{_}3561{_}To say that he is _supercilious_ means that he maintains toward others an attitude of lofty indifference or sneering contempt.
10073-8{_}3562{_}To say that he is _insolent_ means that he is purposely and perhaps coarsely disrespectful toward others, especially toward his superiors.
10073-8{_}3563{_}To say that he is _insulting_ means that he gives or offers personal affront, probably in scornful or disdainful speech.

10073-8{_}3657{_}Does he sing with his lips closed?
10073-8{_}3658{_}You say that he _hums_.
10073-8{_}3659{_}Does he utter the short, perhaps sharp, notes of certain birds and insects?
10073-8{_}3660{_}You say that he _chirps_ or _chirrups_.
10073-8{_}3661{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: <trill, pipe, quaver, peep, cheep, twitter>.
10073-8{_}3662{_}_Sentences_: A cricket ____ in the grass outside the door.
10073-8{_}3663{_}He abstractedly gazed out of the window and ____ a few strains of an old song.
10073-8{_}3664{_}Listen, they are ____ the Te Deum.
10073-8{_}3665{_}"And ____ still dost soar, and soaring ever ____."
10073-8{_}3666{_}A strange, uncanny blending of false and true notes it is when the Swiss mountaineers are ____.
10073-8{_}3667{_}Negroes, as a race, love to ____.
10073-8{_}3668{_}As she soothes the child to sleep she ____ a "rock-a-bye-baby."
10073-8{_}3669{_}<Suave, bland, unctuous, fulsome, smug>.
10073-8{_}3670{_}_Suave_ implies agreeable persuasiveness or smooth urbanity.
10073-8{_}3671{_}_Bland_ suggests a soothing or coaxing kindness of manner, one that is sometimes lacking in sincerity.
10073-8{_}3672{_}_Unctuous_ implies excessive smoothness, as though one's manner were oiled.
10073-8{_}3673{_}The word carries a decided suggestion of hypocrisy.
10073-8{_}3674{_}_Fulsome_ suggests such gross flattery as to be annoying or cloying.
10073-8{_}3675{_}_Smug_ suggests an effeminate self-satisfaction, usually not justified by merit or achievement.
10073-8{_}3676{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: <complaisant, elegant, trim, dapper, spruce, genteel, urbane, well-bred, gracious, affable, benign>.
10073-8{_}3677{_}_Sentences_: He thought his answer exceedingly brilliant and settled back into his chair with ____ complacency.
10073-8{_}3678{_}"____ the smile that like a wrinkling wind On glassy water drove his cheek in lines."
10073-8{_}3679{_}They were irritated by his ____ praise.

10073-8{_}3682{_}____ as a diplomat.
10073-8{_}3683{_}<Talkative, loquacious, garrulous, fluent, voluble, glib>.
10073-8{_}3684{_}(This group may be contrasted with the _Silent_ group, above.)
10073-8{_}3685{_}A little while ago you were in a crowded room and made a study of the persons disposed to silence.
10073-8{_}3686{_}But your study was carried on under difficulties, for many of those about you showed a tendency to copious or excessive speech.
10073-8{_}3687{_}One woman entered readily into conversation with you and convinced you that her natural disposition was to converse a great deal.
10073-8{_}3688{_}She was _talkative_.
10073-8{_}3689{_}From her you escaped to a man who soon proved that he talked too much and could run on with an incessant flow of words, perhaps employing many of them where a few would have sufficed.
10073-8{_}3690{_}He was _loquacious_.
10073-8{_}3691{_}The two of you were joined by an old gentleman who forthwith began to talk wordily, tediously, continuously, with needless repetitions and in tiresome detail; you suspected that he had suffered a mental decline from age, and that he might be excessively fond, in season and out of season, of talking about himself and his opinions.
10073-8{_}3692{_}He was _garrulous_.
10073-8{_}3693{_}You broke away from these two and fell into the hands of a much more agreeable interlocutor.
10073-8{_}3694{_}He talked with a ready, easy command of words, so that his discourse _flowed_ smoothly.
10073-8{_}3695{_}He was _fluent_.
10073-8{_}3696{_}He introduced you to a lady whose speech possessed smoothness and ease in too great degree; it fairly _rolled_ along, as a hoop does downhill.
10073-8{_}3697{_}The lady was _voluble_.
10073-8{_}3698{_}Into your triangular group broke a newcomer whose speech had in it a flippant, or at least a superficially clever, fluency.
10073-8{_}3699{_}He was _glib_.
10073-8{_}3700{_}Leaving these three to fight (or talk) it out as best they might, you grabbed your hat and hurried outside for a fresh whiff of air.
10073-8{_}3701{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: <chattering, long-winded, prolix, wordy, verbose>.
10073-8{_}3702{_}_Sentences_: The insurance agent was so ____ a talker that I was soothed into sleepiness by his voice.
10073-8{_}3703{_}The ____ old man could talk forever about the happenings of his boyhood.
10073-8{_}3704{_}Through ____ descriptions of life in the city the dapper summer boarder entranced the simple country girl.
10073-8{_}3705{_}I met a ____ fellow on the train, and we had a long conversation.
10073-8{_}3706{_}She was so ____ that I spent half the afternoon with her and learned nothing.
10073-8{_}3707{_}<Weak, debilitated, feeble, infirm, decrepit, impotent>.
10073-8{_}3708{_}_Weak_ is the general word for that which is deficient in strength.
10073-8{_}3709{_}_Debilitated_ is used of physical weakness, in most instances brought on by excesses and abuses.
10073-8{_}3710{_}_Feeble_ denotes decided or extreme weakness, which may excite pity or contempt.
10073-8{_}3711{_}_Infirm_ is applied to a person whose weakness or feebleness is due to age.
10073-8{_}3712{_}_Decrepit_ is used in reference to a person broken down or worn out by infirmities, age, or sickness.
10073-8{_}3713{_}_Impotent_ implies such loss or lack of strength or vitality as to render ineffective or helpless.
10073-8{_}3714{_}_Assignment for further discrimination_: <enervated, languid, frail>.
10073-8{_}3715{_}_Sentences_: "Here I stand, your slave, A poor, ____, weak, and despis'd old man."

10073-8{_}3718{_}A fellow who does that has a[n] ____ mind.
10073-8{_}3719{_}He staggered about trying to strike his opponent, but rage and his wound rendered him for the time ____.
10073-8{_}3720{_}The grasp of the old man was so ____ that the cup trembled in his hand.
10073-8{_}3721{_}"Like rich hangings in a homely house, So was his will in his old ____ body."
10073-8{_}3722{_}After his long illness he was as ____ as a child.
10073-8{_}3723{_}He made but a[n] ____ attempt to defend himself.
10073-8{_}3724{_}<Wise, learned, erudite, sagacious, sapient, sage, judicious, prudent, provident, discreet>.
10073-8{_}3725{_}(Compare the distinction between _knowledge_ and _wisdom_ under Words Often Confused above.)
10073-8{_}3726{_}_Wise_ implies sound and discriminating judgment, resulting from either learning or experience.
10073-8{_}3727{_}_Learned_ denotes the past acquisition of much information through study.
10073-8{_}3728{_}_Erudite_ means characterized by extensive or profound knowledge.
10073-8{_}3729{_}_Sagacious_ implies far-sighted judgment and intuitive discernment, especially in practical matters.
10073-8{_}3730{_}_Sapient_ is now of infrequent use except as applied ironically or playfully to one having or professing wisdom.
10073-8{_}3731{_}_Sage_ implies deep wisdom that comes from age or experience.
10073-8{_}3732{_}_Judicious_ denotes sound judgment or careful discretion in weighing a matter with reference to its merits or its consequences.
10073-8{_}3733{_}_Prudent_ conveys a sense of cautious foresight in judging the future and planning for it upon the basis of the circumstances at hand.
10073-8{_}3734{_}_Provident_ suggests practical foresight and careful economy in preparing for future needs.
10073-8{_}3735{_}_Discreet_ denotes care or painstakingness in doing or saying the right thing at the right time, and the avoidance thereby of errors or unpleasant results.

10073-8{_}3749{_}To spend every cent of one's income is surely not to be ____.
10073-8{_}3750{_}<Work, labor, toil, drudgery>.
10073-8{_}3751{_}All of us, at times anyhow, get out of as much work as we can.
10073-8{_}3752{_}We even use the word _work_ and its synonyms loosely and indolently.
10073-8{_}3753{_}Perhaps this is a literary aspect of the labor problem.
10073-8{_}3754{_}If, however, we can shake off our sluggishness and exert ourselves in discriminating our terms, we shall use _work_ as a general word for effort, physical or mental, to some purposive end; _labor_ for hard, physical work; _toil_ for wearying or exhaustive work; and _drudgery_ for tedious, monotonous, or distasteful work, especially of a low or menial kind.

10073-8{_}3763{_}VIII      SYNONYMS IN LARGER GROUPS (2)   You have now seen enough of the method of discriminating synonyms to take more of the responsibility for such work upon yourself.
10073-8{_}3764{_}In this chapter, therefore, the plan followed in Exercise A is abandoned and no discriminations are supplied you.
10073-8{_}3765{_}EXERCISE B  For some of the generic words in Exercise A you will find antonyms in Exercise C. Here is a list:  In Exercise A: walk, laugh, busy, hate, masculine, old  In Exercise C: run, cry, idle, love, feminine, young.
10073-8{_}3766{_}Now each of the generic terms in C is followed by a list of its synonyms.
10073-8{_}3767{_}But for the six generic terms just given let us see how many synonyms you can find for yourself.
10073-8{_}3768{_}Simply study each word in turn, think of all the synonyms for it you can summon, strike out those you consider far-fetched.
10073-8{_}3769{_}Then compare your list with the list under the antonym in Exercise A; if possible, improve your list by means of this comparison.
10073-8{_}3770{_}Finally, compare your revised list with the list in Exercise C.  In Exercise C are two generic terms that carry the same idea (but not in the same part of speech) as generic terms in Exercise A.
10073-8{_}3771{_}They are as follows:  In Exercise A: sing, death  In Exercise C: song, die.
10073-8{_}3772{_}Take _song_ and _die_.
10073-8{_}3773{_}First, find all the satisfactory synonyms you can for yourself.
10073-8{_}3774{_}Then if possible improve your list by studying the list under the corresponding word in Exercise A.
10073-8{_}3775{_}Finally, compare your revised list with the one in Exercise C.   EXERCISE C  After three introductory groups (dealing with thoroughly concrete ideas and words) the synonyms in this exercise are arranged alphabetically according to the first word in each group.

10073-8{_}4480{_}EXERCISE D  In this exercise each group of synonyms is followed by quotations from authoritative writers in which the words are discriminatingly employed.
10073-8{_}4481{_}Find the meaning of each italicized word in these quotations, and differentiate the word accurately from the others in that group.
10073-8{_}4482{_}Substitute for it other words from the group, and observe precisely how the meaning is affected.
10073-8{_}4483{_}(So many of the quotations are from poetry that these will be printed as verse rather than, as in the preceding exercises, in continuous lines like prose.)
10073-8{_}4484{_}<Affront, insult, indignity.>    A moral, sensible, and well-bred man   Will not _affront_ me,--and no other can.
10073-8{_}4485{_}An old _affront_ will stir the heart     Through years of rankling pain.
10073-8{_}4486{_}The way to procure _insults_ is to submit to them.
10073-8{_}4487{_}A man meets with no more respect than he exacts.
10073-8{_}4488{_}It is often better not to see an _insult_ than to avenge it.
10073-8{_}4489{_}Even a hare, the weakest of animals, may _insult_ a dead lion.
10073-8{_}4490{_}To a native of rank, arrest was not merely a restraint, but a foul personal _indignity_.
10073-8{_}4491{_}<Dishonor, disgrace, ignominy, infamy, obloquy, opprobrium>.
10073-8{_}4492{_}His honor rooted in _dishonor_ stood,   And faith unfaithful kept him falsely true.
10073-8{_}4493{_}It is hard to say which of the two we ought most to lament,--the unhappy man who sinks under the sense of his _dishonor_, or him who survives it.
10073-8{_}4494{_}Could he with reason murmur at his case   Himself sole author of his own _disgrace_?
10073-8{_}4495{_}Whatever _disgrace_ we may have deserved, it is almost always in our power to re-establish our character.
10073-8{_}4496{_}When in _disgrace_ with fortune and men's eyes   I all alone beweep my outcast state.
10073-8{_}4497{_}Their generals have been received with honor after their defeat; yours with _ignominy_ after conquest.
10073-8{_}4498{_}Wilful perpetuations of unworthy actions brand with most indelible characters of _infamy_ the name and memory to posterity.
10073-8{_}4499{_}And when his long public life, so singularly chequered with good and evil, with glory and _obloquy_, bad at length closed forever, it was to Daylesford that he retired to die.
10073-8{_}4500{_}Great _opprobrium_ has been thrown on her name.
10073-8{_}4501{_}<Fame, honor, renown, glory, distinction, reputation, repute, celebrity, eminence, notoriety>.
10073-8{_}4502{_}Let _fame_, that all hunt after in their lives,   Live register'd upon our brazen tombs.
10073-8{_}4503{_}Men have a solicitude about _fame_; and the greater share they have of it, the more afraid they are of losing it.
10073-8{_}4504{_}_Fame_ is no plant that grows on mortal soil,   .
10073-8{_}4505{_}.
10073-8{_}4506{_}.
10073-8{_}4507{_}.
10073-8{_}4508{_}.
10073-8{_}4509{_}.
10073-8{_}4510{_}.
10073-8{_}4511{_}.
10073-8{_}4512{_}But lives and spreads aloft by those pure eyes   And perfect witness of all-judging Jove;   As he pronounces lastly on each deed,   Of so much _fame_ in heaven expect thy meed.
10073-8{_}4513{_}When faith is lost, when _honor_ dies,   The man is dead.
10073-8{_}4514{_}Act well your part; there all the _honor_ lies.
10073-8{_}4515{_}The Athenians erected a large statue of Aesop, and placed him, though a slave, on a lasting pedestal, to show that the way to _honor_ lies open indifferently to all.
10073-8{_}4516{_}I could not love thee, dear, so much,   Loved I not _honor_ more.
10073-8{_}4517{_}That nation is worthless which does not joyfully stake everything on her _honor_.
10073-8{_}4518{_}By heaven methinks it were an easy leap   To pluck bright _honor_ from the pale-fac'd moon.
10073-8{_}4519{_}That merit which gives greatness and _renown_ diffuses its influence to a wide compass, but acts weakly on every single breast.
10073-8{_}4520{_}Speak no more of his _renown_,   Lay your earthly fancies down,   And in the vast cathedral leave him,   God accept him, Christ receive him.
10073-8{_}4521{_}The young warrior did not fly; but met death as he went forward in his strength.
10073-8{_}4522{_}Happy are they who dies in youth, when their _renown_ is heard!
10073-8{_}4523{_}The paths of _glory_ lead but to the grave.
10073-8{_}4524{_}_Glory_ long has made the sages smile; 'tis something, nothing, words, illusion, wind.
10073-8{_}4525{_}Not once or twice in our rough island-story   The path of duty was the way to _glory_.
10073-8{_}4526{_}He was a charming fellow, clever, urbane, free-handed, with all that fortunate quality in his appearance which is known as _distinction._  Never get a _reputation_ for a small perfection if you are trying for _fame_ in a loftier area.
10073-8{_}4527{_}One may be better than his _reputation_ or his conduct, but never better than his principles.
10073-8{_}4528{_}I see my _reputation_ is at stake   My _fame_ is shrewdly gor'd.
10073-8{_}4529{_}CASSIO.
10073-8{_}4530{_}_Reputation, reputation, reputation!_ O!
10073-8{_}4531{_}I have lost my reputation.
10073-8{_}4532{_}I have lost the immortal part of myself, and what remains is bestial.
10073-8{_}4533{_}My reputation, Iago, my reputation!
10073-8{_}4534{_}IAGO.
10073-8{_}4535{_}As I am an honest man, I thought you had received some bodily wound.
10073-8{_}4536{_}You have a good _repute_ for gentleness and wisdom.
10073-8{_}4537{_}_Celebrity_ sells dearly what we think she gives.
10073-8{_}4538{_}Kings climb to _eminence_    Over men's graves.
10073-8{_}4539{_}_Notoriety_ is short-lived; _fame_ is lasting.
10073-8{_}4540{_}<Hatred, hate, animosity, ill-will, enmity, hostility, bitterness, malice, malevolence, malignity, rancor, resentment, dudgeon, grudge, spite>.
10073-8{_}4541{_}The _hatred_ we bear our enemies injures their happiness less than our own.
10073-8{_}4542{_}_Hate_ is like fire; it makes even light rubbish deadly.

10073-8{_}4545{_}No place is so propitious to the formation either of close friendships or of deadly _enmities_ as an Indiaman.
10073-8{_}4546{_}There need be no _hostility_ between evolutionist and theologian.
10073-8{_}4547{_}Shall we be thus afflicted in his wreaks,   His fits, his frenzy, and his _bitterness?_    Speak of me as I am; nothing extenuate,   Nor set down aught in _malice_.

10073-8{_}4549{_}His flight is occasioned rather by the _malignity_ of his countrymen than by the enmity of the Egyptians.
10073-8{_}4550{_}Where the soul sours, and gradual _rancor_ grows,   Imbitter'd more from peevish day to day.
10073-8{_}4551{_}Peace in their mouthes, and all _rancor_ and vengeance in their hartes [hearts].
10073-8{_}4552{_}For them the gracious Duncan have I murder'd;   Put _rancors_ in the vessel of my peace   Only for them.
10073-8{_}4553{_}Her _resentment_ against the king seems not to have abated.
10073-8{_}4554{_}Mrs. W. was in high _dudgeon_; her heels clattered on the red-tiled floor, and she whisked about the house like a parched pea upon a drum-head.
10073-8{_}4555{_}If I can catch him once upon the hip,   I will feed fat the ancient _grudge_ I bear him.
10073-8{_}4556{_}Men of this character pursue a _grudge_ unceasingly, and never forget or forgive.
10073-8{_}4557{_}And since you ne'er provoked their _spite_,   Depend upon't their judgment's right.
10073-8{_}4558{_}<Marriage, matrimony, wedlock>.
10073-8{_}4559{_}(With this group compare the _matrimonial_ group in Exercise C, above.)
10073-8{_}4560{_}_Marriages_ are made in heaven.
10073-8{_}4561{_}Hasty _marriage_ seldom proveth well.
10073-8{_}4562{_}A man finds himself seven years older the day after his _marriage_.
10073-8{_}4563{_}Let me not to the _marriage_ of true minds   Admit impediments.
10073-8{_}4564{_}_Marriage_ is the best state for man in general; and every man is a worse man in proportion as he is unfit for the married state.
10073-8{_}4565{_}_Matrimony_--the high sea for which no compass has yet been invented.
10073-8{_}4566{_}_Wedlock's_ a lane where there is no turning.
10073-8{_}4567{_}What is _wedlock_ forced, but a hell,   An age of discord and continual strife?
10073-8{_}4568{_}<Mercy, clemency, lenity, leniency, lenience, forbearance>.
10073-8{_}4569{_}Teach me to feel another's woe,   To hide the fault I see;   That _mercy_ I to others show,   That _mercy_ show to me.
10073-8{_}4570{_}The quality of _mercy_ is not strain'd,   It droppeth as the gentle rain from heaven   Upon the place beneath: it is twice bless'd;   It blesseth him that gives and him that takes;        *       *       *       *       *   And earthly power doth then show likest God's   When _mercy_ seasons justice.
10073-8{_}4571{_}_Clemency_ is the surest proof of a true monarch.
10073-8{_}4572{_}_Lenity_ will operate with greater force, in some instances, than vigor.
10073-8{_}4573{_}All the fellows tried to persuade the Master to greater _leniency_, but in vain.
10073-8{_}4574{_}It will be necessary that this acceptance should be followed up by measures of the utmost _lenience_.
10073-8{_}4575{_}There is however a limit at which _forbearance_ ceases to be a virtue.
10073-8{_}4576{_}<Pity, sympathy, compassion, commiseration, condolence>.
10073-8{_}4577{_}Careless their merits or their faults to scan,   His _pity_ gave ere charity began.
10073-8{_}4578{_}For _pity_ melts the mind to love.
10073-8{_}4579{_}For _pitee_ renneth [runneth] soon in gentle herte [heart].
10073-8{_}4580{_}Our _sympathy_ is cold to the relation of distant misery.
10073-8{_}4581{_}Man may dismiss _compassion_ from his heart, but God will never.
10073-8{_}4582{_}It is unworthy a religious man to view an irreligious one either with alarm or aversion; or with any other feeling than regret, and hope, and brotherly _commiseration_.
10073-8{_}4583{_}Their congratulations and their _condolences_ are equally words of course.
10073-8{_}4584{_}<Poverty, want, need, destitution, indigence, penury>.
10073-8{_}4585{_}Is there for honest _poverty_   That hings [hangs] his head, and a' that?
10073-8{_}4586{_}Not to be able to bear _poverty_ is a shameful thing, but not to know how to chase it away by work is a more shameful thing yet.
10073-8{_}4587{_}Stitch!
10073-8{_}4588{_}stitch!
10073-8{_}4589{_}stitch!
10073-8{_}4590{_}In _poverty_, hunger, and dirt,   And still with a voice of dolorous pitch,   Would that its tone could reach the Rich,   She sang this "Song of the Shirt!"
10073-8{_}4591{_}_Poverty_ is dishonorable, not in itself, but when it is a proof of laziness, intemperance, luxury, and carelessness; whereas in a person that is temperate, industrious, just and valiant, and who uses all his virtues for the public good, it shows a great and lofty mind.
10073-8{_}4592{_}_Want_ is a bitter and hateful good,   Because its virtues are not understood;   Yet many things, impossible to thought,   Have been by _need_ to full perfection brought.
10073-8{_}4593{_}Hundreds would never have known _want_ if they had not first known waste.
10073-8{_}4594{_}O!
10073-8{_}4595{_}reason not the _need_; our basest beggars   Are in the poorest thing superfluous:   Allow not nature more than nature needs,   Man's life is cheap as beast's.
10073-8{_}4596{_}The Christian inhabitants of Thessaly would be reduced to _destitution_.
10073-8{_}4597{_}It is the care of a very great part of mankind to conceal their _indigence_ from the rest.
10073-8{_}4598{_}Chill _penury_ repress'd their noble rage,   And froze the genial current of the soul.
10073-8{_}4599{_}Chill _penury_ weighs down the heart itself; and though it sometimes be endured with calmness, it is but the calmness of despair.
10073-8{_}4600{_}Where _penury_ is felt the thought is chain'd,   And sweet colloquial pleasures are but few.
10073-8{_}4601{_}<Regret, compunction, remorse, contrition, penitence, repentance>.
10073-8{_}4602{_}_Regrets_ over the past should chasten the future.
10073-8{_}4603{_}He acknowledged his disloyalty to the king with expressions of great _compunction_.
10073-8{_}4604{_}Through no disturbance of my soul,   Or strong _compunction_ in me wrought,   I supplicate for thy control.
10073-8{_}4605{_}God speaks to our hearts through the voice of _remorse_.
10073-8{_}4606{_}To err is human; but _contrition_ felt for the crime distinguishes the virtuous from the wicked.
10073-8{_}4607{_}Christian _penitence_ is something more than a thought or an emotion or a tear; it is action.
10073-8{_}4608{_}_Repentance_ must be something more than mere _remorse_ for sins; it comprehends a change of nature befitting heaven.
10073-8{_}4609{_}<Stubborn, obstinate, pertinacious, intractable, refractory, contumacious>.
10073-8{_}4610{_}For fools are _stubborn_ in their way,   As coins are harden'd by th' allay;   And _obstinacy's_ ne'er so stiff   As when 'tis in a wrong belief.
10073-8{_}4611{_}They may also laugh at their _pertinacious_ and incurable obstinacy.
10073-8{_}4612{_}He who is _intractable_, he whom nothing can persuade, may boast himself invincible.
10073-8{_}4613{_}There is a law in each well-order'd nation   To curb those raging appetites that are   Most disobedient and _refractory_.
10073-8{_}4614{_}He then dissolved Parliament, and sent its most _refractory_ members to the Tower.
10073-8{_}4615{_}If he were _contumacious_, he might be excommunicated, or, in other words, be deprived of all civil rights and imprisoned for life.

10073-8{_}4935{_}Arrange the intervening words in an ascending scale.
10073-8{_}4936{_}Dark, bright              Wet, dry Savage, civilized         Beautiful, ugly Friend, enemy             Hope, despair Wise, foolish             Love, hate Enormous, minute          Admirable, abominable Curse, bless              Pride, humility    IX      MANY-SIDED WORDS   In Chapter VII you made a study of printed distinctions between synonyms.
10073-8{_}4937{_}In Chapter VIII you were given lists of synonyms and made the distinctions yourself.
10073-8{_}4938{_}Near the close of Chapter VIII you were given words and discovered for yourself what their synonyms are.
10073-8{_}4939{_}This third stage might seem to reveal to you the full joys and benefits of your researches in this subject.
10073-8{_}4940{_}Certainly to find a new word for an old one is an exhilarating sort of mental travel.
10073-8{_}4941{_}And to find a new word which expresses exactly what an old one expressed but approximately is a real acquisition in living.
10073-8{_}4942{_}But you are not yet a perfectly trained hunter of synonyms.
10073-8{_}4943{_}Some miscellaneous tasks remain; they will involve hard work and call your utmost powers into play.
10073-8{_}4944{_}Of these tasks the most important is connected with the hint already given that many words, especially if they be generic words, have two or more entirely different meanings.
10073-8{_}4945{_}Let us first establish this fact, and afterwards see what bearing it has on our study of synonyms.
10073-8{_}4946{_}My friend says, "I hope you will have a good day."
10073-8{_}4947{_}Does he mean an enjoyable one in general?
10073-8{_}4948{_}a profitable or lucrative one, in case I have business in hand?
10073-8{_}4949{_}a successful one, if I am selling stocks or buying a house?
10073-8{_}4950{_}Possibly he means a sunshiny day if I intend to play golf, a snowy day if I plan to go hunting, a rainy day if my crops are drying up.
10073-8{_}4951{_}The ideas here are varied, even contradictory, enough; yet _good_ may be used of every one of them.
10073-8{_}4952{_}_Good_ is in truth so general a term that we must know the attendant circumstances if we are to attach to it a signification even approximately accurate.
10073-8{_}4953{_}This does not at all imply that _good is_ a term we may brand as useless.
10073-8{_}4954{_}It implies merely that when our meaning is specific we must set _good_ aside (unless circumstances make its sense unmistakable) in favor of a specific word.
10073-8{_}4955{_}_Things is_ another very general term.
10073-8{_}4956{_}In "Let us wash up the things" it likely means dishes or clothes.
10073-8{_}4957{_}In "Hang your things in the closet" it likely means clothes.
10073-8{_}4958{_}In "Put the things in the tool-box" it likely means tools.
10073-8{_}4959{_}In "Put the things in the sewing-basket" it likely means thread, needles, and scissors.
10073-8{_}4960{_}In "The trenches are swarming with these things" it likely means cooties.
10073-8{_}4961{_}A more accurate word is usually desirable.
10073-8{_}4962{_}Yet we may see the value of the generality in the saying "A place for everything, and everything in its place."
10073-8{_}4963{_}_Good_ and _things_ are not alone in having multitudinous meanings.
10073-8{_}4964{_}There are in the language numerous many-sided words.
10073-8{_}4965{_}These words should be studied carefully.
10073-8{_}4966{_}True, they are not always employed in ambiguous ways.
10073-8{_}4967{_}For example, _right_ in the sense of correct is seldom likely to be mistaken for _right_ in the sense of not-left, but a reader or hearer may frequently mistake it for _right_ in the sense of just or of honorable.
10073-8{_}4968{_}In the use of such words, therefore, we cannot become too discriminating.
